Zakat Payment Claim: Livestock vs. Cash
Ruling on belief of owner's claim of zakat payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the treatment of livestock and cash when the owner claims to have paid zakat to the poor, and state the reason for the difference.

Core Idea
Livestock zakat is collected by the state; cash and trade zakat is delegated to the owner.

Zakat on livestock (sa'imah) is typically collected by an appointed collector (musaddiq), so the owner cannot simply claim he paid the poor directly. However, for cash and trade goods, the owner is trusted because the distribution is left to his discretion. This passage from al-Quduri's matn draws a clear line between these two categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The owner says: 'I have paid the zakat to the poor.'
This is a claim after the fact.
2
Classify the type of wealth
Determine whether the wealth is livestock (mawashi), produce (ushr), or cash/trade goods.
Livestock and ushr are treated alike; cash and trade goods are treated alike.
3
Apply the ruling
If livestock or ushr, the owner is not believed and must pay again. If cash or trade goods, he is believed.
The rationale: payment of zakat on hidden wealth is delegated to the owner.
4
Memorize the distinction
Commit to memory: 'For livestock and crops, the claim is rejected; for dirhams, dinars, and trade goods, it is accepted.'
This is a key rule in zakat transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال : قد أديتها إلى الفقراء : لم يُصدق، وتُؤخذ منه ثانياً. وكذلك هذا الخلاف في العشر. وإن كان المالُ ،دراهم، أو دنانير، أو أموال التجارة، فقال: قد أديتها إلى الفقراء: صدق؛ لأن دفع زكاة هذه الأموال مفوض إلى أربابها.
And if he says: 'I have paid it to the poor,' he is not believed, and it is taken from him a second time. And likewise this dispute is in tithe (zakat of crops). And if the wealth is dirhams, or dinars, or trade goods, and he says: 'I have paid it to the poor,' he is believed; because the payment of zakat on these properties is delegated to their owners.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a critical distinction in Hanafi zakat law regarding who is believed about payment. For livestock (sa'imah), if the owner claims to have paid zakat directly to the poor, he is not believed and must pay again. This is because zakat on livestock is typically collected by the state collector (musaddiq), so the owner cannot discharge it privately unless unable. The text adds that the same dispute applies to tithe (العشر, zakat of agricultural produce). However, for cash (dirhams, dinars) and trade goods ('urud al-tijarah), the owner is believed if he claims to have paid the poor directly. The rationale is that the payment of zakat on these types is delegated (مفوض) to the owners; they are expected to distribute it themselves, unlike livestock where the collector's involvement is customary. This ruling highlights the distinction between zakat on manifest wealth (الظاهر) like livestock and crops, which is collected by the state, and zakat on hidden wealth (الباطن) like cash and trade goods, which is left to individual discretion.

Key Terms
العشر (al-'ushr): The zakat on agricultural produce, typically one-tenth.
أموال التجارة (amwal al-tijarah): Trade goods, inventory intended for sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the owner believed when he claims to have paid zakat to the poor for trade goods but not for livestock?
A) Because trade goods are more valuable
B) Because payment of zakat on trade goods is delegated to the owner
C) Because livestock zakat is not considered obligatory
D) Because the collector does not exist for trade goods

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi zakat, the owner is not believed for livestock and crops but is believed for cash and trade goods because the latter are delegated to the owner's discretion.

Ruling on Claiming Zakat Payment to a Different Collector or to the Poor
Ruling on evidentiary credibility of zakat payment claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the scenarios where a zakat payer's claim of payment is accepted with an oath, and where it is rejected, according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A claim of zakat payment is believed only if paid to an available collector, not to the poor, and a discharge is not required in the relied-upon view.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a practical scenario: a person who claims to have already paid the zakat on livestock or crops. The ruling hinges on to whom the payment was made and whether the collector exists. The Hanafi school holds a nuanced position that balances the burden of proof with the principle of trusting the claimant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Scenarios
The passage covers payment to another collector (where another collector exists), payment to a collector where none exists, and payment directly to the poor.
Each scenario has a different ruling.
2
Determine the Ruling for Each Scenario
Read the text: for payment to an existing collector, believed with oath; for non-existing collector, not believed; for payment to poor, not believed.
The oath is part of the procedure.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
A weaker narration requires a written discharge (barā'ah) for credibility. The Zahir al-Riwayah does not require it.
The mufta bihi is the position without discharge.
4
Recognize the Authority Weight
The text explicitly says 'fī ẓāhir ar-riwāyah' (in the apparent narration), indicating this is the authoritative position.
The weaker narration is not relied upon in practice.
5
Apply to 'Ushr
The same rulings apply to zakat on grains and fruits ('ushr).
Extension is explicitly mentioned in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الإبل صدق وإن قال: قد أديتها إلى مصدق غيرك : إن كان هناك مصدق غيره : مع يمينه، سواء أتى بالبراءة أم لا، في ظاهر الرواية. وروي: أنه لا يُصدق حتى يأتي بها. وإن لم يكن هناك مصدق: لم يُصدق. وإن قال : قد أديتها إلى الفقراء : لم يُصدق، وتُؤخذ منه ثانياً. وكذلك هذا الخلاف في العشر.
Chapter of Zakat of Camels: He is believed if he says: I have paid it to a collector other than you: if there is another collector: with his oath, whether he brings a discharge or not, in the apparent narration. And it is narrated: that he is not believed until he brings it. And if there is no [other] collector: he is not believed. And if he says: I have paid it to the poor: he is not believed, and it is taken from him a second time. And similarly this difference applies to the tithe.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a person claims to have already paid the zakat on camels. The ruling is that if he claims to have paid it to a different official collector (musaddiq), and there is indeed another collector in the area, he is believed with an oath, regardless of whether he produces a written discharge. This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah. A weaker narration states that he must produce the discharge to be believed. If no other collector exists, he is not believed, because payment should have been made to the known collector. If he claims to have paid it directly to the poor (fugara'), he is not believed and must pay again, because the obligation to pay the collector remains. This same ruling applies to zakat on grains and fruits ('ushr).

Key Terms
مصدق (muṣaddiq): The official collector appointed by the state to collect zakat on livestock and crops.
البراءة (al-barā'ah): A written document from the collector confirming receipt of zakat, serving as a discharge.
في ظاهر الرواية (fī ẓāhir ar-riwāyah): The well-known and authoritative transmission from the founding imams, constituting the primary position of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, if a person claims to have paid zakat on camels to a different official collector and there is another collector in the area, what is required for him to be believed?
A) He must produce a written discharge from the collector.
B) He is believed with an oath, whether he brings a discharge or not.
C) He must have two witnesses to prove payment.
D) He is not believed under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a claim of zakat payment is only accepted with oath if paid to an existing collector; payment to the poor or to a non-existing collector requires re-payment.

Zakat on Cows: Nisab, Conditions, and the Tabi' Animal
Ruling on nisab and zakat animal for cows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab for cows, list the conditions for zakat, and explain the definition of tabi' and the intra-Hanafi dispute on its age.

Core Idea
The nisab for cows is 30, provided they are grazing and a year passes; on exactly 30, one tabi' (yearling) is due.

This passage opens the chapter on zakat of cows from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, establishing the core ruling: zakat becomes due only when one owns thirty grazing cows for a full lunar year. The animal to be given is called 'tabi'' – a calf that has completed its first year and entered the second, so named because it still follows its mother. The passage also contains a glimpse of an intra-Hanafi difference regarding the minimum age of the animal, which we will explore.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The passage rules that zakat is obligatory on cows under certain conditions. The overarching hukm is fard, as zakat is a pillar of Islam.
2
List the Conditions for Zakat on Cows
Three conditions are mentioned: (1) nisab – at least 30 cows; (2) sa'imah – they must be grazing livestock; (3) haul – a full lunar year must pass.
The Hanafi school only requires zakat on sa'imah (grazing) animals, not on ma'lufah (stall-fed).
3
Define the Zakat Animal: Tabi'
When exactly 30 cows are owned, the zakat due is one tabi' (male) or tabi'ah (female). A tabi' is a calf that has completed one year and entered the second.
The name tabi' comes from its habit of following its mother.
4
Understand the Gender Rule
For cows and sheep, both male and female are acceptable, unlike camels where a male is not accepted except by valuation.
This is because the male is not deficient in value for cows and sheep.
5
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Minimum Age
The text indicates that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the minimum age is a tabi' (one year). It then cuts off, suggesting Abu Hanifa may have required a different age (e.g., a two-year-old).
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is the view of the Sahibayn, i.e., one year.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة البقر ليس في أقل من ثلاثين من البقر السائمة صدقة. فإذا كانت ثلاثين سائمةً، وحالَ عليها الحولُ : ففيها تبيع، أو تبيعةٌ. قال رحمه الله : : (ليس في أقل من ثلاثين من البقر صدقة، فإذا كانت ثلاثين سائمة، وحال عليها الحولُ: ففيها تبيع، أو تبيعة). وهو: الذي له سنةٌ، وطعن في الثانية، سمي: تبيعاً؛ لأنه إلى الآن يتبع أمه. ثم الأنثى لا تزيد على الذكر في هذا الباب، وكذا في الغنم. بخلاف الإبل : حيث لا يجوز الذكر فيها إلا على طريق القيمة. وأدنى من تتعلق بها الزكاة في البقر: تبيع عندهما، وقال أبو...
Chapter of Zakat of Cows: There is no charity on less than thirty grazing cows. So when there are thirty grazing [cows], and a year passes over them: then in them is a tabi' or tabi'ah. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (There is no charity on less than thirty cows, so when there are thirty grazing and a year passes over them: then in them is a tabi' or tabi'ah). And it is: that which has one year and has entered the second; it is called tabi' because it still follows its mother. Then the female does not exceed the male in this chapter, and likewise in sheep. Unlike camels, where the male is not allowed except by way of value. And the minimum [age] for which zakat is due on cows is a tabi' according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and Abu [Hanifa said ...]
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on zakat of cows. The matn states the basic ruling: there is no zakat on fewer than thirty grazing cows. When one possesses exactly thirty cows that have been grazing (sa'imah) for a full lunar year, the zakat due is one tabi' (a one-year-old calf) or its female counterpart (tabi'ah). The commentator explains that a tabi' is a calf that has completed one year and entered the second, and is called tabi' because it still follows its mother. The ruling applies equally to male and female calves; there is no preference for female as in camels. The text then begins an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the minimum age for zakat liability on cows is a tabi'. Abu Hanifa differs (the text cuts off, likely then stating his view that it must be a tabi' ? or a different age). The passage also notes that for camels, the male is not acceptable in zakat except when valued; for cows and sheep, both male and female are acceptable.

Key Terms
سائمة (sā'imah): Livestock that graze on natural pasture for most of the year, as opposed to being stall-fed.
تبيع (tabī'): A bovine calf that has completed one year and entered the second; literally 'one who follows' (its mother).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nisab (minimum number) of grazing cows for zakat to become obligatory?
A) 1 cow
B) 5 cows
C) 30 cows
D) 40 cows

Key Takeaway
Zakat on cows becomes fard at 30 heads, provided they are sa'imah and a full year passes; on exactly 30, one tabi' (yearling) is due, and the male is equally acceptable as the female.

Zakat on 40 Cattle: Obligation and Permissible Substitution
Ruling on the obligatory zakat for forty cattle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory zakat for forty cows and identify the permissible alternative of giving two tabi'.

Core Idea
For forty cattle, one two-year-old cow (musinnah) is due; but two one-year-old bulls (tabi') may substitute.

This lesson explains the basic zakat obligation for cattle when the herd reaches forty heads. The classical Hanafi text specifies the exact animal required, while the commentator expands on a permissible concession that provides flexibility for the owner. Understanding this ruling helps you apply zakat correctly in common pastoral settings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on zakat for forty cattle. State the obligation: one musinnah (or musin) must be given.
The matn says 'وفي أربعين : مُسِنَّةٌ، أو مُسِنٌ'.
2
Understand the definition of musinnah
The commentator defines musinnah as a cow that has completed two years and entered the third. This is the standard Hanafi threshold.
Compare with other schools which may require a different age.
3
Analyze the permissible substitution
The text permits giving two tabi' (one-year-olds) instead of one musinnah. The legal reasoning is qiyas by first awla: if they suffice for sixty cattle, they more certainly suffice for forty.
This shows that zakat can be discharged with equivalent or superior value.
4
Evaluate the flexibility
Recognize that the core obligation is strict, but substitution is allowed as a concession, reflecting the facilitative nature of Hanafi fiqh.
The owner may choose the easier option if it benefits the recipient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة البقر
وفي أربعين : مُسِنَّةٌ، أو مُسِنٌ.
قوله: (وفي أربعين مُسِنَّةٌ، أو مسن)، وهي: ما لها سنتان، وطعنت في الثالثة..
فإن أعطى تبيعين: جاز؛ لأنهما يُجزيان عن الستين، فلأن يجزيان عما دونها : أولى.
Chapter on Zakat of Cattle. And for forty: a musinnah (female cow two years old) or a musin (male). His statement: (And for forty: a musinnah or musin), and it is one that has two years and has entered the third. If he gives two tabi' (young bulls of one year), it is permissible because they suffice for sixty, so it is more appropriate that they suffice for less than that.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This is a ruling from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar on the zakat of cattle. The original text states that when one owns forty head of cattle (cows/buffaloes), the zakat due is one musinnah (a cow that has completed two years and entered the third), or optionally a male musin of the same age. The commentator (Ibn al-Shihna) explains that 'musinnah' is defined as having two years and having entered the third year. He then adds a permissive ruling: if the owner gives two tabi' (a young bull that has completed one year and entered the second) instead of one musinnah, this is valid and permissible. The reasoning given is that two tabi' are accepted for sixty cows (as will be explained later), so they are even more suitable for forty cows, which is a lesser obligation. This ruling indicates flexibility in discharging zakat, as long as the value is equivalent or better from the perspective of the recipient. Note that the basic obligation is one musinnah, but substitution with two tabi' is allowed as a concession.

Key Terms
مُسِنَّة (musinnah): A cow that has completed two years and entered the third year of life.
تبيع (tabi'): A young bull that has completed one year and entered the second year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the zakat obligation for 40 cows?
A) One tabi'
B) One musinnah
C) Two musinnahs
D) One taby' and one musinnah

Key Takeaway
For forty cattle, the zakat is one musinnah (two-year-old cow), but two one-year-old bulls (tabi') are a valid substitute.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Zakat on Cattle Between 40 and 60
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fractional zakat calculation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on zakat for 40-60 cows and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has three positions on fractional zakat; the mufta bihi is that no zakat is due until the next nisab.

This passage reveals a fascinating internal disagreement within the Hanafi school over whether zakat on cows should be calculated proportionally between the fixed nisab thresholds. Understanding how the school resolved this dispute—preferring the easier, non-fractional view of the Sahibayn—illustrates how practical convenience can tip the scale when multiple valid narrations exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the numerical range in dispute
The disagreement concerns cattle numbers between 40 and 60. Below 40 and above 60, all Hanafi imams agree on the standard nisab rules.
Note that the agreed schedule for cows: 1-39: no zakat; 40-59: disputed; 60: 2 tabi'; 70-79: 1 tabi' + 1 musinnah, etc.
2
State the original riwayah (Abu Hanifa)
The original narration from Abu Hanifa requires proportional fractions for each additional cow beyond 40 up to 60. For example, 41 cows: 1/40 of a musinnah; 42 cows: 1/20 of a musinnah, etc.
This is 'riwayat al-asl' transmitted through Imam Muhammad.
3
State the riwayah of Hasan ibn Ziyad
A second narration from Abu Hanifa, reported by Hasan ibn Ziyad (nawadir), holds that nothing is due until 50, then a musinnah + 1/4 musinnah or 1/3 tabi' becomes due. This is based on treating the 'awqas' (gaps) as ninths.
This riwayah is less authoritative (nawadir) and not used in fatwa.
4
State the Sahibayn position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that no zakat is due on any number between 40 and 59. At 60, two tabi' become due. They reject fractional payments as impractical.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn view, chosen for ease and to avoid fractional calculations. The original riwayah is acknowledged but not operative in fatwa.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that in cases of ikhtilaf, the easier position is often preferred for general practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فإذا زادت على الأربعين وجب في الزيادة بقدر ذلك، إلى ستين عند أبي حنيفة. ففي الواحدة ربعُ عُشر مُسِنَّة، وفي الثنتين: نصف عشر مُسنَّة، وفي الثلاثة : ثلاثة أرباع عُشر مُسِنَّة ، وفي الأربع: عُشْرُ مُسنة). وهذه رواية الأصل، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة: أنه لا يجب في الزيادة شيء حتى تبلغ خمسين، فيكون فيها: مسنة وربع مسنة، أو ثلث تبيع، لأن الأوقاص في البقر : تسع تسع. وقالا : لا شيء في الزيادة حتى تبلغ ستين : فيكون فيها تبيعان، أو تبيعتان . ولا خلاف بينهم فيما دون الأربعين، ولا فيما وراء الستين.
His statement: (If it increases beyond forty, then upon the increase there is due proportionally up to sixty according to Abu Hanifa. So for one extra: one-fourth of a tenth of a musinnah; for two: half a tenth of a musinnah; for three: three-fourths of a tenth of a musinnah; for four: one-tenth of a musinnah). This is the original narration. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa: nothing is due on the increase until it reaches fifty, then there is a musinnah and one-fourth of a musinnah, or one-third of a tabi', because the fractions in cattle are 'nine-ninths'. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: nothing is due on the increase until it reaches sixty, then there is two tabi' or two tabi'at. And there is no disagreement among them concerning what is below forty, nor what is above sixty.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on how to calculate zakat when the number of cattle is between 40 and 60. The original ruling (zahir al-riwayah) attributed to Abu Hanifa requires that for every extra cow beyond 40 up to 60, a proportional fraction of a musinnah is due. For example, if there are 41 cows, one must give one-fourth of one-tenth of a musinnah (i.e., 1/40 of a musinnah). This is based on the principle that the zakat is calculated proportionally. However, there is a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad (nawadir) that according to Abu Hanifa, nothing is due until 50, and then the obligation becomes 1 musinnah + 1/4 musinnah or 1/3 tabi'. This is because the gaps (awqas) in cattle are considered as 'nine-ninths' meaning the intervals are treated as fractional parts. The two senior disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), hold that nothing extra is due until the total reaches 60, at which point two tabi' are due. They do not require any payment for numbers between 40 and 59. Importantly, all three imams agree on the ruling for numbers below 40 (i.e., no zakat) and for 60 and above (the schedule is agreed). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the view of the Sahibayn for practical convenience, as it avoids fractional payments.

Key Terms
أوقاص (awqas): Singular: waqs. The gaps or intervals between fixed nisab numbers in zakat on cattle. Here used to explain the fractional calculation.
رواية الأصل (riwayat al-asl): The original/primary narration from Abu Hanifa, as opposed to the riwaya of Hasan ibn Ziyad (which is nawadir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school, what is the zakat on 45 cows?
A) 1/4 of a musinnah
B) 1/10 of a musinnah
C) Nothing; zakat is due only at 60
D) 1 musinnah

Key Takeaway
For zakat on cattle between 40 and 60, the Hanafi mufta bihi is that no fractional zakat is due; the obligation begins at the next nisab (60).

Zakat on Cattle: Schedule from 70 Onwards and the Buffalo Ruling
Agreed schedule for zakat on cattle from 70 upwards and buffalo equated to cattle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zakat due for any number of cattle from 70 upwards using the agreed Hanafi schedule and apply the rule that buffaloes are equivalent to cattle.

Core Idea
For every ten cows from 70, zakat alternates between tabi' and musinnah by consensus.

This passage from Al-Jawhara Al-Nayyirah presents the fixed schedule for zakat on cattle when the herd reaches 70 or more. The schedule is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams and provides a predictable pattern that continues indefinitely. Additionally, the text establishes that buffaloes fall under the same rulings as cattle for zakat, an important clarification for regions where buffaloes are common.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the base schedule for 70-120
The text gives specific combinations for each ten from 70 to 120. Write them down and note the alternating pattern: for 70: one musinnah + one tabi'; 80: two musinnahs; 90: three tabi'; 100: two tabi' + one musinnah; 110: two musinnahs + one tabi'; 120: four tabi' or three musinnahs.
The pattern repeats every 30? Actually check the alternation.
2
Understand the alternating pattern
Notice that the required animal type changes every ten: from 70 to 80, the number of musinnahs increases; from 80 to 90, it shifts to tabi's. The commentator says 'on this analogize', meaning you can continue the pattern for any number beyond 120.
The key is the alternation every ten between tabi' (1-year-old) and musinnah (2-year-old) in a balanced way.
3
Apply the buffalo rule
The text explicitly states that buffaloes (jawamis) are the same as cattle (baqar) for zakat. So the schedule applies identically to buffalo herds.
This is important for farmers in regions with buffaloes.
4
Recognize the consensus
The schedule is by consensus (ijma') among the Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. There is no intra-madhhab dispute on these amounts.
Other schools may have different schedules, but within the Hanafi school, this is settled.

Commentary
This passage delineates the agreed-upon schedule for zakat on cattle from 70 heads upwards. All Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) are unanimous on these amounts. For 70 cows, the zakat is one musinnah and one tabi'. For 80: two musinnahs. For 90: three tabi'. For 100: two tabi' and one musinnah. The pattern continues such that every ten cows, the type of animal alternates between musinnah and tabi'. The commentator extends the schedule to 110 and 120, and instructs to analogize beyond that. The final line establishes the rule that buffaloes (جواميس) are treated identically to cattle (بقر) for zakat purposes. This is important as buffaloes were common in some regions. The schedule is based on the principle that for every nisab increase, the zakat due is a combination of musinnahs and tabi's in a fixed pattern, alternating every ten. This is by consensus (الإجماع) among the Hanafi founders.

Key Terms
جواميس (jawamis): Singular: jamus. Buffaloes. The text equates them with cattle (baqar) for zakat rulings.

Zakat on Cows: Dispute on Increments and Ruling on Buffaloes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on increment zakat and equating buffaloes to cows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi zakat schedule for cows from 30 to 120, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the increment from 40 to 60, and apply the equivalence of buffaloes to cows in rulings of zakat, sacrifice, and riba.

Core Idea
Buffaloes are like cows for zakat and riba, but custom may exclude them from oaths.

Zakat on cattle follows fixed thresholds and specific animals. In this passage, we learn the schedule for cows from 30 to 120, including a dispute between the founders of the Hanafi school on whether an increment between 40 and 60 triggers zakat. The passage also clarifies that buffaloes are treated identically to cows for most rulings, but oaths are interpreted by custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify agreed thresholds
List the zakat due at each threshold: 30 cows (tabi'), 40 (musinnah), 60 (two tabi's), 70 (musinnah + tabi'), etc.
No disagreement below 40 or above 60.
2
Analyze the increment dispute
Focus on the range 40-60 cows. Abu Hanifa requires one musinnah on 50 cows; Sahibayn require nothing until 60.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
3
Understand the alternating pattern
Observe that every ten cows from 60 onward alternates between tabi' and musinnah payments.
This pattern is by consensus.
4
Study buffalo equivalence
Buffaloes are equated to cows in zakat, sacrifice, and riba rulings due to shared species.
Oaths depend on custom and wording.
5
Apply oath distinctions
Distinguish between swearing by 'cow meat' vs 'a cow' vs 'cows' based on customary usage.
If buffalo is abundant locally, oaths may include it.

Chapter of Zakat on Cows. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Nothing is due on the increase (from 40 to 60) until it reaches sixty: then there are two calves (tabi') or two heifers. At seventy: one Musinnah and one tabi'. At eighty: two Musinnahs. At ninety: three tabi's. At one hundred: two tabi's and one Musinnah. On this basis the obligation changes every ten, from tabi' to Musinnah, and from Musinnah to tabi'. And buffaloes and cows are the same. * His statement: (And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: nothing on the increase until it reaches sixty: then two tabi's or two tabi'ahs). There is no disagreement among them regarding below forty, nor regarding above sixty. * His statement: (At seventy: one Musinnah and one tabi', at eighty: two Musinnahs, at ninety: three tabi's, at one hundred: two tabi's and one Musinnah). At one hundred and ten: two Musinnahs and one tabi', at one hundred and twenty: four tabi's or three Musinnahs, and on this analogize. * His statement: (And on this the obligation changes every ten, from tabi' to Musinnah, and from Musinnah to tabi', and this is by consensus. * His statement: (And buffaloes and cows are the same). Meaning in zakat, sacrifice, and consideration of riba. As for oaths: if one swears not to eat cow meat, he does not break his oath by eating buffalo, due to lack of custom and its rarity in our land, so the oath does not include it. But if it is abundant in a place, it should be that he breaks his oath. Thus in al-Nihaya. And if one swears not to buy cows (al-baqar), it does not include buffaloes. And if he swears not to buy a cow (baqaran), it includes them, and he breaks his oath by buying them because the definite article (alif lam) indicates the customary.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses the zakat schedule for cows and the status of buffaloes. The author first presents the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) regarding the increment between 40 and 60 cows. According to them, on the increase from 40 to 60—i.e., 50 cows—nothing is due. Only when the herd reaches 60 does zakat become wajib with two tabi' (one-year-old calves). Abu Hanifa differed, holding that on 50 cows, one Musinnah (a two-year-old) is due. The text then outlines the agreed-upon schedule from 60 upward: 70 cows require one Musinnah and one tabi'; 80 require two Musinnahs; 90 require three tabi's; 100 require two tabi's and one Musinnah. The pattern alternates every ten cows, switching between tabi' and Musinnah. The commentator notes that there is no ikhtilaf for numbers below 40 or above 60. The passage then states that buffaloes (jawaamis) are equivalent to cows (baqar) in three rulings: zakat, sacrifice ('udhiyah), and the prohibition of riba. However, for oaths, the custom and common understanding determine whether buffalo is included. If one swears not to eat cow meat, buffalo is not included because it is rare in the scholars' lands; but if it becomes common locally, the oath would be broken. Similarly, if the oath uses the generic plural 'al-baqar' (cows), it does not include buffaloes; but if it uses the indefinite singular 'baqaran' (a cow), it does include buffaloes due to the customary usage. This nuanced treatment reflects Hanafi reliance on 'urf (custom) in interpreting oaths.

Key Terms
تبيع (tabi'): A calf that has completed one year and entered the second.
مسنة (musinnah): A cow that has completed two years and entered the third.
جواميس (jawaamis): Buffaloes; considered the same species as domestic cattle for zakat, sacrifice, and riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two tabi's
B) Two musinnahs
C) One musinnah and one tabi'
D) Three tabi's

Key Takeaway
Zakat on cows follows a precise schedule of every ten, and buffaloes are legally equivalent except where custom governs oaths.

Zakat on Sheep: Nisab and Age Requirement
Ruling on nisab and age condition for zakat on sheep/goats with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum nisab for zakat on grazing sheep and goats, identify the age requirement (thani), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding lambs.

Core Idea
Zakat on sheep requires a minimum of forty animals that have reached the age of thani (one year old); lambs under that age are not zakatable.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah addresses the foundational rules for zakat on sheep and goats. It establishes the nisab (minimum threshold) of forty animals and introduces a key age requirement: only animals that have reached the age of thani (having completed one year) are subject to zakat. An internal Hanafi disagreement on this age condition highlights the importance of textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab
The matn states: 'There is no charity in less than forty sheep.' So the minimum number for zakat to be due is forty sheep or goats.
This nisab is absolute regardless of gender.
2
Establish the age requirement
The commentator explains that the zakatable animal must be a thani, which is defined as the one that has completed a full year (hawl). Younger animals are called himalan (lambs) and are not zakatable.
Thani literally means two-year-old, but the school applies it to animals that have completed one year of life.
3
Apply the rule to lambs
If a person owns forty lambs under one year old, no zakat is due according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, because they have not reached the required age.
This is the majority Hanafi position.
4
Understand Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that zakat is due even on lambs if they reach the nisab, based on the general command without age restriction. However, the mufta bihi is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: whether one must give zakat on forty lambs or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الغنم
قدم الغنم على الخيل ؛ لكثرتها، وكون زكاة الغنم متفقاً فيها، وزكاة الخيل مختلفاً فيها.
ثم الغنم يقع على الذكور والإناث، وعليهما جميعاً.
* قال رحمه الله: (ليس في أقل من أربعين شاة: صدقة). أدنى السن التي تجب فيه الزكاة: الثني، فصاعداً، وهو: الذي أتى عليه حول عندهما، وما دونه: حملان، لا شيء فيها.
وعند أبي يوسف: تجب فيها الزكاة.
Chapter of Zakat of Sheep/Goats. He gave precedence to sheep over horses because of their abundance and because zakat on sheep is agreed upon, while zakat on horses is disputed. Then the term 'ghanam' applies to both males and females, and the ruling applies to both. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'There is no charity in less than forty sheep.' The minimum age for zakat to be obligatory is the thani (two-year-old) and above; that is the one that has completed one year according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Below that are lambs (himalan); there is nothing due on them. According to Abu Yusuf, zakat is due on them.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on zakat of sheep and goats (ghanam). The commentator first explains why the author placed this chapter before horses: sheep are more common and zakat on them is a matter of consensus, whereas zakat on horses is disputed. The term 'ghanam' includes both males and females. Then the matn is quoted: 'There is no charity in less than forty sheep.' The commentator clarifies that the zakatable animal must have reached the age of thani (at least two years old, i.e., having completed one year of life from birth?). Actually, 'thani' means an animal that has completed its second year and entered the third; but the text says 'the one that has completed a year (hawl)' according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Younger animals (himalan) are not subject to zakat. However, Abu Yusuf holds that zakat is due even on lambs under one year if they reach the nisab. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, i.e., age requirement stands.

Key Terms
الغنم (al-ghanam): Sheep and goats collectively; the passage confirms it includes both males and females.
الثني (al-thani): An animal that has completed its second year and entered the third; in this context, it means the animal must have passed one full year of life.
حملان (himalan): Lambs (young sheep) that have not yet reached the age of thani; they are not subject to zakat according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires zakat on sheep only when the nisab of forty is reached and each animal has reached the age of thani (one year old); lambs under that age are exempt.

Zakat of Sheep: Nisab, Scale, Age Requirements, and Gender Rules
Expands the matn of Al-Quduri on zakat of sheep, clarifying nisab, age, and gender.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi nisab and graduated scale for sheep zakat, identify the required age (thani), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding lambs and the cross-madhhab difference on gender.

Core Idea
Zakat on sheep begins at 40 head, with a graduated scale; the animal given must be at least one year old (thani), and both males and females are acceptable.

This passage from Al-Jawhara Al-Nayyira commemorates the key rulings of zakat on sheep. It details the minimum nisab, the sliding scale up to 400 and beyond, the condition that the sheep must be sa'imah and one year old (thani), and addresses the intra-Hanafi difference on lambs and the cross-madhhab difference on gender. Understanding these details ensures correct calculation and fulfillment of this fard obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab threshold and conditions
Zakat on sheep is obligatory only when the number reaches 40, the sheep are sa'imah (grazing freely for most of the year), and a full lunar year has passed. Note that lambs (under one year) do not count toward the nisab.
The condition of sa'imah is unique to livestock; hand-fed animals are not subject to zakat.
2
Apply the graduated scale
Memorize the brackets: 40-120 head: 1 sheep; 121-200: 2; 201-399: 3; 400: 4; then for every additional 100, add 1 sheep. This scale applies regardless of the gender mix.
The scale begins at 40; below that, no zakat.
3
Determine the required age for the given animal
The animal given must be a thani (one year old and entering its second year). Lambs (jadha') are not accepted. Note the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf allows lambs, but the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require thani, and this is the mufta bihi.
A narration from Abu Hanifa permits jadha' from sheep (da'n) by analogy with udhiya, but the apparent view (zahir al-riwayah) is that jadha' is not taken.
4
Recognize the gender rule
Both male and female sheep are acceptable for zakat. The Hanafi school allows males, contra Imam al-Shafi'i who permits males only if the entire flock consists of males.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a Hanafi may give a male even when females are present; a Shafi'i must give a female if available.
5
Apply the rule for mixed flocks
If the flock contains both sheep (da'n) and goats (ma'z), they are treated as one category for nisab. Zakat is taken from the predominant type; if equal, the owner chooses.
This rule simplifies administration and avoids hardship.

باب زكاة الغنم
ليس في أقل من أربعين شاة صدقة.
فإذا كانت أربعين سائمة، وحالَ عليها الحولُ : ففيها شاة، إلى مائة وعشرين .
فإذا زادت واحدةً : ففيها شاتان، إلى مائتين .
باب زكاة الغنم
قدم الغنم على الخيل ؛ لكثرتها، وكون زكاة الغنم متفقاً فيها، وزكاة الخيل مختلفاً فيها.
ثم الغنم يقع على الذكور والإناث، وعليهما جميعاً.
* قال رحمه الله: (ليس في أقل من أربعين شاة: صدقة). أدنى السن التي تجب فيه الزكاة: الثني، فصاعداً، وهو: الذي أتى عليه حول عندهما، وما دونه: حملان، لا شيء فيها.

فإذا زادت واحدةً: ففيها شاتان، إلى مائتين.

فإذا بلغت أربعمائة : ففيها أربع شياه .
ثم في كل مائة شاة.
فإذا زادت واحدةً : ففيها ثلاث شياه إلى ثلاثمائة وتسعة وتسعين. فإذا بلغت أربعمائة : ففيها أربع شياه، ثم في كل مائة شاة). وصفتها الثني، فصاعداً، وهي: ما لها سنة، وطعنت في الثانية،
ولا يؤخذ الجذع.
والضأن والمعز في ذلك سواء.
وعن أبي حنيفة : أن الجذع من الضأن يجوز، وهو: ما أتى عليه أكثر السنة؛ لأنه يجوز في الأضحية وهي أضيق من الزكاة، ألا ترى أن التبيع لا يجوز فيها، ويجوز في الزكاة، والأول هو الظاهر.
ويؤخذ في زكاة الغنم الذكور والإناث.
وقال الشافعي ) : لا يؤخذ الذكر إلا إذا كانت كلها ذكوراً.
ـ ثم السنة أن النصاب إذا كان ضأناً: يؤخذ من الضأن.
وإن كان معزاً: فمن المعز.
وإن كان منهما فمن الغالب. وإن كانا سواء: فمن أيهما شاء.
Chapter on Zakat of Sheep.
There is no charity (zakat) on less than forty sheep.
When they are forty grazing (sa'imah) and a year has passed: then one sheep is due, up to one hundred and twenty.
When it increases by one: then two sheep are due, up to two hundred.
Chapter on Zakat of Sheep.
Sheep were mentioned before horses due to their abundance, and because zakat on sheep is agreed upon, while zakat on horses is disputed.
Then 'ghanam' (sheep) applies to males and females, and on both of them together.
* He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (There is no charity on less than forty sheep). The minimum age for zakat to be obligatory is the thani (second-year animal) and above, which is: one that has completed a year according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and what is below that: lambs, nothing is due on them. And according to Abu Yusuf: zakat is due on them.
* His statement: (When they are forty grazing, and a year has passed: then one sheep is due up to one hundred and twenty.
When it increases by one: then two sheep are due, up to two hundred.
When it increases by one: then three sheep are due, up to three hundred and ninety-nine.
When it reaches four hundred: then four sheep are due.
Then for every hundred: one sheep.
When it increases by one: then three sheep are due up to three hundred and ninety-nine. When it reaches four hundred: then four sheep are due, then for every hundred: one sheep). Its description is thani (second-year) and above, which is: one that has had a year and entered the second, and the jadha' (young one) is not taken.
Sheep (da'n) and goats (ma'z) are equal in that regard.
And from Abu Hanifa: that the jadha' from sheep (da'n) is permissible, which is: one that has had most of the year; because it is permissible in udhiya (sacrifice), and it is more restrictive than zakat. Do you not see that the tabi' (young of cow) is not permissible in it, but is permissible in zakat. And the first (opinion) is the apparent one.
And in zakat of sheep, males and females are taken.
And al-Shafi'i said: Males are not taken unless all of them are males.
Then the sunna (ruling) is: if the nisab is all da'n, then from da'n is taken. If it is all ma'z, then from ma'z. If it is from both, then from the predominant. And if they are equal, then from whichever he wishes.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the chapter of Zakat of Sheep from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. It opens by stating the general ruling that no zakat is due on less than forty sheep. The commentator then explains that the minimum age of the sheep for zakat is the thani (an animal that has completed one year and entered the second), according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Below that age, the animals are considered lambs, and no zakat is due. However, Abu Yusuf holds the opposing view that zakat is due even on lambs below the age of one year. The dominant position (zahir al-riwaya) is the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and it is the relied-upon position.

The passage then presents the graduated scale for zakat on sheep: 40 to 120 head: one sheep; 121 to 200: two sheep; 201 to 399: three sheep; 400: four sheep; and thereafter for every hundred additional head, one sheep. The animal given must be a thani or older; the jadha' (young animal that has not yet reached the second year) is not accepted. However, a narration from Abu Hanifa permits the jadha' from sheep (da'n) because it is accepted for udhiya (sacrifice), which has stricter conditions than zakat. The commentator notes that the first opinion (that jadha' is not taken) is the apparent one.

Regarding gender, both males and females can be taken, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i's view that males should only be taken if the entire flock consists of males. Finally, the rule for a mixed flock of sheep and goats: if the flock is entirely one type, zakat is taken from that type; if mixed, from the predominant; if equal, from whichever the owner chooses.

Key Terms
سائمة (sa'imah): Livestock that graze freely for most of the year on natural pasture, as opposed to being fed at home.
ثني (thani): An animal that has completed one year and entered the second year.
جذع (jadha'): A young animal that has not yet reached the second year, often around six to eight months old.
ضأن (da'n): Sheep (as opposed to goats).
معز (ma'z): Goats.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the zakat due on 350 sa'imah sheep owned for one year?
A) 2 sheep
B) 3 sheep
C) 4 sheep
D) 1 sheep

Key Takeaway
Zakat on sheep begins at 40 head, requires the animal to be at least one year old (thani), both genders acceptable, and follows a graduated scale up to 400 then per 100.

Nisab of Sheep and the Required Age (Thani vs Jadha)
Ruling on nisab and age requirement with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab thresholds for sheep, identify the required age (thani), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of jadha.

Core Idea
For zakat on sheep, the nisab starts at 40, and the animal taken must be a thani — a jadha is not accepted, except in a riwaya from Abu Hanifa.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the specific rulings for zakat on sheep, including the nisab thresholds and the age of the animal. It also presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about whether a jadha (first-year sheep) can be given, with Abu Hanifa allowing it based on qiyas with udhiyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab thresholds
Read the Arabic carefully. Note that from 40 to 120 sheep, one sheep is due. Then for 121-200, two; 201-300, three; 301-399, three; at 400, four; then for every hundred, one. The text specifically states: 'When it increases by one (to 301), three sheep are due, up to 399. When it reaches 400, four sheep are due.'
The matn is concise; memorize these numbers.
2
Note the required animal: thani
The text says: 'Its description is the thani (second-year) and above, which is one that has completed a year and entered the second. The jadha (first-year) is not taken.' So the minimum age is one year plus entering the second.
Thani is defined as a sheep that has completed one year and entered the second.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on jadha
The text reports from Abu Hanifa that the jadha from da'n (sheep) is permissible. The author then states that the first opinion (not taking jadha) is the apparent (al-zahir) one, indicating that the mufta bihi is to not take jadha.
Abu Hanifa's view is a riwaya; it is not the relied-upon position in the madhhab.
4
Analyze the qiyas argument from udhiyah
Abu Hanifa's proof is qiyas: Jadha is permissible in udhiyah, and udhiyah is stricter (adyaq) than zakat because the tabi' calf is not allowed in udhiyah but is in zakat. Therefore, if jadha is allowed in the stricter, it should be allowed in the less strict (zakat).
This is a qiyas jaliy. The objector might argue that the illa is not valid, but the Hanafi response highlights the disparity between the two obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الغنم
فإذا زادت واحدةً : ففيها ثلاث شياه، إلى ثلاثمائة وتسعة وتسعين .
فإذا بلغت أربعمائة : ففيها أربع شياه .
ثم في كل مائة شاة.
فإذا زادت واحدةً : ففيها ثلاث شياه إلى ثلاثمائة وتسعة وتسعين. فإذا بلغت أربعمائة : ففيها أربع شياه، ثم في كل مائة شاة). وصفتها الثني، فصاعداً، وهي: ما لها سنة، وطعنت في الثانية،
ولا يؤخذ الجذع.
والضأن والمعز في ذلك سواء.
وعن أبي حنيفة : أن الجذع من الضأن يجوز، وهو: ما أتى عليه أكثر السنة؛ لأنه يجوز في الأضحية وهي أضيق من الزكاة، ألا ترى أن التبيع لا يجوز فيها، ويجوز في الزكاة، والأول هو الظاهر.
ويؤخذ في زكاة الغنم الذكور والإناث.
وقال الشافعي : لا يؤخذ الذكر إلا إذا كانت كلها ذكوراً.
ـ ثم السنة أن النصاب إذا كان ضأناً: يؤخذ من الضأن.
وإن كان معزاً: فمن المعز.
وإن كان منهما فمن الغالب. وإن كانا سواء: فمن أيهما شاء.
والضأن والمعز سواء .
قوله : ( والضأن والمعز: سواء): يعني في وجوب الزكاة، واعتبار الربا، وجواز الأضحية.
أما لو حلف لا يأكل لحم الضأن، فأكل لحم المعز: لا يحنث.
Chapter on Zakat of Sheep. When it increases by one (to 301), three sheep are due, up to 399. When it reaches 400, four sheep are due. Then for every hundred, one sheep. Its description is the thani (second-year) and above, which is one that has completed a year and entered the second. The jadha (first-year) is not taken. Sheep and goats are equal in that. From Abu Hanifa: that the jadha from da'n (sheep) is permissible, which is one that has passed most of the year; because it is permissible in udhiyah (sacrifice), and udhiyah is stricter than zakat. Do you not see that the tabi' (one-year-old calf) is not permissible in it (udhiyah), but is permissible in zakat. The first (opinion) is the apparent one. In zakat of sheep, both males and females are taken. Al-Shafi'i said: the male is not taken unless all are males. Then the sunna is that if the nisab is da'n, take from da'n; if it is ma'z (goats), take from ma'z; if from both, then from the majority; if they are equal, then from whichever one wishes. And sheep and goats are equal. He said: (sheep and goats: equal) means in the obligation of zakat, consideration of riba, and permissibility of udhiyah. If one swears not to eat lamb (da'n) meat and eats goat (ma'z) meat: he is not in breach.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar covers the rulings for zakat on sheep (ghanam). It first states the nisab thresholds: from 40 to 120 sheep, one sheep is due; from 121 to 200, two sheep; 201 to 300, three; 301 to 399, three; 400, four; then for every hundred thereafter, one sheep. The required animal is the thani (a sheep that has entered its second year). The jadha (one that has not yet completed its first year) is not accepted, except according to a transmission from Abu Hanifa that the jadha from da'n (sheep) is acceptable. His proof is qiyas: since the jadha is permissible in udhiyah (sacrificial animal), and udhiyah has stricter conditions than zakat (e.g., the tabi' calf is not allowed in udhiyah but is in zakat), then it should also be permissible in zakat. The commentary notes that the first opinion (not accepting jadha) is the apparent one. Next, the passage states that both male and female sheep are taken in zakat. Imam al-Shafi'i held that males are taken only if the entire flock is male; otherwise, females are taken. The Hanafi position is that males are also acceptable. Finally, when the nisab consists of both da'n and ma'z (goats), the animal taken should be from the majority; if equal, the owner may choose. The commentary clarifies that the ruling 'sheep and goats are equal' applies to zakat, riba, and udhiyah, but not to oaths—if one swears not to eat da'n, eating ma'z does not break the oath.

Key Terms
ثني (thani): A sheep that has completed one year and entered its second year.
جذع (jadha): A sheep that has not yet completed one year; in this text, jadha from da'n is not taken in zakat according to the apparent position.
ضأن (da'n): Sheep (specifically the species Ovis aries); as opposed to ma'z (goats).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Jadha (first-year)
B) Thani (second-year)
C) Any age
D) Only male sheep

Key Takeaway
The nisab for sheep starts at 40, and the animal must be a thani, though Abu Hanifa allows jadha from da'n.

Taking Males and Females in Zakat on Sheep (Hanafi vs Shafi'i)
Inter-madhhab dispute on gender of zakat animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether male sheep are taken in zakat, and state the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Hanafis take both males and females; Shafi'is take males only if the entire flock is male.

This short passage presents a clear inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school allows both male and female sheep to be given as zakat, while Imam al-Shafi'i restricts males to cases where the entire flock is male. Understanding this difference is essential for knowing which animal to give in mixed flocks.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The text says: 'ويؤخذ في زكاة الغنم الذكور والإناث' — both males and females are taken. This is the general rule.
No further qualification is given; males are equally acceptable.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds: 'لا يؤخذ الذكر إلا إذا كانت كلها ذكوراً' — the male is not taken unless all are males. He argues that females are more valuable for breeding.
This is an inter-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In a mixed flock (males and females), a Hanafi can give a male, but a Shafi'i must give a female. If all are males, both schools take a male.
The practical consequence dictates which animal is acceptable.

Mixing Da'n and Ma'z: Same Rulings but Different Oaths
Ruling on mixing sheep and goats with an exception for oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when nisab consists of both sheep and goats, the animal taken is from the majority, and distinguish between rulings on zakat/riba/udhiyah and oaths.

Core Idea
Sheep and goats are equal in zakat, riba, and udhiyah, but if one swears not to eat lamb, eating goat does not break the oath.

This passage clarifies that while da'n (sheep) and ma'z (goats) are treated identically for zakat, riba, and udhiyah, they are considered different species for the purpose of oaths. When the nisab is mixed, the animal taken for zakat should be from the majority species. This demonstrates that legal equality does not imply identity in all contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for mixed flocks
If the nisab is da'n, take from da'n; if ma'z, take from ma'z; if both, take from the majority; if equal, choose any.
This applies to the zakat animal taken.
2
Note the general equality statement
The text says: 'والضأن والمعز سواء' — they are equal. The commentary specifies this applies to zakat, riba, and udhiyah.
This is a general principle in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Identify the exception for oaths
The commentary adds: if one swears not to eat da'n, eating ma'z does not break the oath. This shows that equality does not extend to linguistic categories.
The Arabic word for sheep (da'n) does not include goats (ma'z) in common usage.

Ruling: Sheep and Goats Are Equal in Zakat, Riba, and Udhiyah
Ruling statement with expansion on three specific applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three fiqh rulings where sheep and goat are treated identically in the Hanafi school and identify the exception concerning oaths.

Core Idea
Sheep and goat are one genus for zakat, riba, and udhiyah, but distinct for oaths.

The Hanafi school treats sheep (ضأن) and goat (معز) as the same species for several important legal obligations. This equivalence simplifies calculation in zakat, prevents riba in exchange, and permits either for udhiyah. However, when it comes to oaths, the customary distinction prevails — a nuance you must understand to apply the rulings correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three areas of equality
The passage states that sheep and goat are equal in (1) obligation of zakat, (2) consideration of riba, (3) permissibility of udhiyah.
Each area has its own hukm: zakat is fard, riba is haram, udhiyah is sunnah muakkadah.
2
Understand the rationale
In Hanafi fiqh, both are considered the same genus (جنس واحد) for these transactions, so they combine in nisab and are subject to riba al-fadl rules.
Also for udhiyah, both are valid sacrificial animals.
3
Note the exception for oaths
If someone swears not to eat sheep meat, eating goat does not break the oath because custom distinguishes them.
The principle: in oaths, ‘urf (custom) is considered, not genus.
4
Apply the principle
Recognize that legal identity depends on context; in ibadat (zakat, udhiyah) and muamalat (riba), genus matters; in oaths, customary usage matters.
This is a key distinction: do not automatically extend equality to all rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الغنم والضأن والمعز سواء . قوله : ( والضأن والمعز: سواء): يعني في وجوب الزكاة، واعتبار الربا، وجواز الأضحية. أما لو حلف لا يأكل لحم الضأن، فأكل لحم المعز: لا يحنث.
Chapter of Zakat on Sheep. And the sheep (dha'n) and the goat (ma'z) are the same. His saying: (And the sheep and the goat: are the same): means in the obligation of zakat, and the consideration of riba, and the permissibility of udhiyah. As for if he swore not to eat the meat of sheep, then he ate the meat of goat: he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary explaining the equality of sheep and goats in three areas: first, the obligation of zakat (both are liable for zakat when they reach nisab); second, the consideration of riba (they are considered the same genus for riba al-fadl); third, the permissibility of udhiyah (sacrificial animal). The commentator then adds a fourth nuance: if a person swears an oath not to eat sheep meat, eating goat meat does not break the oath because they are considered different types for the purpose of oaths. This follows the Hanafi principle that in oaths, the customary understanding (urf) is considered, and ordinarily sheep and goat are distinguished.

Key Terms
الضأن (al-da'n): Sheep; a type of livestock
المعز (al-ma'z): Goat; a type of livestock
الربا (al-riba): Usury or interest; specifically in this context riba al-fadl (excess in exchange of same genus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Sheep and goat are the same for zakat, riba, and udhiyah; but for oaths, custom distinguishes them.

Zakat on Horses: Conditions and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
States ruling and conditions for zakat on horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for zakat on horses in the Hanafi school and apply the ruling to different scenarios (male-only, mixed, female-only).

Core Idea
Zakat on horses is wajib only if sa'imah, mixed or female, and after one year; male-only are exempt due to no reproduction.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira addresses the ruling of zakat on horses, a topic that was controversial even among the early Hanafi authorities. The commentator clarifies the conditions and the reasoning behind the exemption of male-only horses, providing insight into the school's method of talil (causal reasoning).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and conditions
What is the act being ruled upon? Zakat on horses. What conditions are necessary for it to be wajib? The horses must be sa'imah (free grazing), a full lunar year must pass (haul), and the herd must be either mixed (males and females) or all females.
The condition of 'mixed or females' is specific to horses.
2
State the ruling
When conditions are met, the owner has a choice: pay one dinar per horse or assess the total value and pay 2.5% (5 dirhams per 200 dirhams).
This is a concession (khiyar) unique to horses.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For male-only horses, there are two narrations (riwayat) within the Hanafi school: one obliges zakat, the other (the sound one) exempts. The commentator declares the second as al-sahih (correct).
The basis for exemption is 'adam al-tanasul' (lack of reproduction).
4
Understand the causal link
Why does gender matter? Zakat is on growing assets (al-amwal al-namiya). Reproduction (tanasul) is the primary form of growth for livestock. Male-only horses do not reproduce, so no zakat. Mixed or female horses can reproduce, so zakat is wajib.
This is a clear example of talil (causal reasoning).
5
Apply to scenarios
Scenario 1: All male horses, sa'imah, one year passes. Ruling: No zakat. Scenario 2: Mixed or all female horses, sa'imah, one year. Ruling: Zakat wajib with the owner's choice.
Always check the condition of sa'imah and haul first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of Zakat on Horses. If horses are sa'ima (pastured freely), males and females, or females, and a year has passed over them: then their owner has the choice: if he wishes, he gives for each horse a dinar, and if he wishes, he assesses their value and gives for every two hundred dirhams five dirhams. And there is no zakat on males alone. Chapter of Zakat on Horses. Its etymology is from al-khuyala, which is pride. And he delayed it because of its rarity and the scarcity of pasturing them, and the difference of opinion concerning the obligation of zakat on them. And the minimum age for zakat to be obligatory on them: that it mounts if it is male, or is mounted if it is female. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: If horses are sa'ima, males and females, or females, and a year has passed: then their owner has the choice: if he wishes, he gives for each horse a dinar, or if he wishes, he assesses them and gives for every two hundred dirhams five dirhams. He only conditioned the mix because concerning males alone there are two narrations: the sound one of them is non-obligation due to lack of reproduction.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on zakat of horses from the commentary. The ruling states: zakat is obligatory on horses only if they are sa'ima (pastured freely) and either mixed males and females or only females, and a year has passed. The owner has a choice: either pay one dinar per horse, or assess the value and pay 2.5% (5 dirhams per 200 dirhams). There is no zakat on male horses alone. The commentator explains why this section was placed late: because horses are rare and there is ikhtilaf on zakat obligation. He also mentions the minimum age: a male must be capable of mounting, a female must be capable of being mounted. The reason for the condition of mixing or females only is that for males alone, there are two narrations within the Hanafi school: the correct one is no obligation because males alone do not reproduce, and reproduction is the basis for increase which is the purpose of zakat.

Key Terms
سائمة (sa'imah): Livestock that graze freely on pasture for most of the year without being fed by the owner.
خيار (khiyar): Option; here the owner has the option to pay either a fixed dinar per horse or a percentage of value.
التناسل (al-tanasul): Reproduction; the basis for zakat obligation because zakat is on growth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The minimum age for zakat is when the horse is one year old.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, zakat on horses is conditional on them being sa'imah, of mixed or female gender, and after a year; male-only horses are exempt due to lack of reproduction.

Zakat on Horses: Conditions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on zakat for horses with intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for zakat on horses according to the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Zakat on horses is obligatory only if grazing, mixed (or female), and not for warfare — but the Sahibayn reject any obligation.

The obligation of zakat on horses is a uniquely Hanafi ruling, subject to specific conditions and a notable intra-madhhab dispute. While Abu Hanifa and Zufar consider it wajib under certain circumstances, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that no zakat is due at all. Understanding the precise conditions and the reasoning behind each view is essential for accurate fatwa on this rare but important issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for zakat on horses
Extract from the passage the three conditions: the horses must be grazing (sa'ima), the herd must be mixed (males and females) or consist only of females, and a full lunar year must pass. Also note the exemption for horses used for warfare.
Males alone are exempt regardless of other conditions.
2
State the owner's payment options
According to Abu Hanifa, the owner may choose to pay one dinar per horse or value the horses and pay 2.5% of their value (5 dirhams per 200 dirhams).
This flexibility is unique to horses.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Zufar rule zakat obligatory; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule no zakat. Identify each side's reasoning: Abu Hanifa relies on analogy with other grazing animals (growth through reproduction), while the Sahibayn see no explicit text and consider horses not for consumption.
The text favors Abu Hanifa's view ('والصحيح: لا بد من الاختلاط').
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Zufar, requiring mixing (ikhtilat) for obligation. For females alone, the stronger view is obligation due to potential reproduction.
Warfare horses are exempt by consensus.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Given a herd of grazing horses that are mixed males and females, not used for warfare, and a year passes, the owner must pay zakat according to the mufta bihi. If the herd consists only of males, no zakat is due.
The niṣāb is two animals (one male, one female) on the preferred view.

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira discusses the chapter on zakat of horses (zakat al-khayl). It begins by stating the ruling: if horses are grazing (sa'ima), mixed male and female or only female, and a full year passes, the owner has a choice: either give one dinar per horse, or value the horses and pay 2.5% of the value (5 dirhams per 200 dirhams). There is no zakat on male horses alone. The commentary notes that the chapter was placed late in the book due to the rarity of the ruling and the difference of opinion (ikhtilāf) among scholars. The minimum age for zakat is when the horse is capable of breeding: a male when it mounts, a female when it is mounted. The condition of mixing (ikhtilāṭ) is necessary because regarding males alone, there is a difference of opinion: the correct view is no zakat because they do not reproduce, unlike other grazing animals which are eaten. For females alone, the more correct view is obligation because they can reproduce with a borrowed stallion, which is commonly available. The book al-Asl by Imam Muhammad states that zakat is only due when both males and females are present, and that the niṣāb (minimum threshold) is two animals (one male, one female). Another narration says zakat is due on males alone, with a niṣāb of one. The correct Hanafi position is that mixing is required. Then the passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that zakat is obligatory on horses, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) say there is no zakat. However, all agree that horses used for warfare (ghazw) are exempt by consensus. For Abu Hanifa and Zufar, the obligation concerns the horse itself, and the value is considered for payment. The text then notes that if the horses do not reach a certain value, the ruling may differ.

Key Terms
خيل (khayl): Horses, specifically the livestock type subject to zakat rules.
سائمة (sa'ima): Grazing livestock that feed themselves on pasture for most of the year.
اختلاط (ikhtilat): Mixing of male and female horses in the herd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) No zakat is due by consensus.
C) Abu Hanifa says obligatory, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no zakat.
D) Only males are subject to zakat.

Key Takeaway
Zakat on horses is wajib only when they are sa'ima, mixed (or female), and not for warfare, following the view of Abu Hanifa and Zufar which is the mufta bihi.

Zakat on Horses: Between Qiyas and Khabar Wahid
Ruling with proof on zakat for horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on zakat for non-war grazing horses, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and evaluate the use of khabar wahid to override qiyas.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa obligates zakat on non-war grazing horses, overriding qiyas with a solitary hadith.

Zakat on livestock is generally straightforward: grazing cattle, sheep, and goats trigger zakat. But what about horses? This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah reveals a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute: while the school's general principle (qiyas) would exempt horses because they are not eaten, Abu Hanifa departs from that analogy based on a prophetic hadith. Understanding how he reconciles this exception with his own usul is key to grasping Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Zufar): zakat is wajib on grazing horses not used for war. The conditions include sā'imah, non-war use, and (in the preferred narration) mixing of males and females. The nisab is either one male horse or one male and one female, depending on the narration.
Zakat is taken from the horse's value, not the animal itself, because horses are not edible.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that no zakat is due on horses at all. This difference stems from their weighing of the hadith and the strength of qiyas.
The Sahibayn follow the stronger qiyas (horses are like mules/donkeys), while Abu Hanifa gives priority to the textual proof.
3
Understand the usul principle: khabar wahid overrides qiyas
Abu Hanifa's principle that a solitary hadith (khabar wahid) can overrule qiyas is the foundational usul move here. The hadith 'For every grazing horse, a dinar or ten dirhams' is weak in transmission, but it is still sufficient in Hanafi methodology to establish a ruling.
This reflects the Hanafi approach: texts with zanni thubut can still produce wujub, and they take precedence over analogical reasoning.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: followers of Abu Hanifa pay zakat on the value of their non-war grazing horses when the nisab is met. Followers of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, or al-Shafi'i pay nothing.
This is a real-world consequence that affects estate planning and annual zakat calculations for horse owners.
5
Identify the mufta bfihi
The author of al-Jawharah al-Nayirah supports Abu Hanifa's view, affirming the necessity of mixing (ikhtilāṭ) and the owner's option to pay in value. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school for fatwa.
Tarjih authority: al-Jawharah al-Nayirah itself; the preference is expressed as 'al-awwal huwa al-zahir' regarding the owner's choice.

Source Text

وفي الإناث المنفردة: روايتان الأصح : الوجوب؛ لأنها تتناسل بالفحل المستعار، والناس لا يتمانعون منه في العادة.

في كل فرس سائمة دينار، أو عشرة دراهم»

Chapter of Zakat on Horses. Contrary to other grazing livestock, where zakat is obligatory on males alone because even if reproduction does not occur, eating occurs. And regarding females alone: there are two narrations; the most correct is obligation because they reproduce through a borrowed stallion, and people usually do not prevent that. It is mentioned in al-Asl that there is nothing [obligatory] on them until they are males and females, and it is not obligatory on males alone nor on females alone because their growth is through reproduction, since they are not edible according to Abu Hanifa. The nisab is two, a male and a female, according to this narration. And it is narrated that it is obligatory on males, so on this the nisab is one. And the correct view is that mixing is necessary. Then the obligation of zakat on horses is only the saying of Abu Hanifa and Zufar. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: nothing is due on them. This is when they are not for war; if they are for war, then nothing is due on them by consensus. Then according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar, the obligation is on the [value] and it is taken from its value, so that if the two horses do not reach [the nisab] according to the narration that stipulates mixing, or the horse [alone] does not reach two hundred dirhams, then an amount in proportion is taken; therefore he said: and if he wishes, he may assess it. His statement: the owner has the choice – by this he avoids the saying of al-Tahawi, for he says the choice is for the agent. And the first is apparent. And his statement: and if he wishes, he may assess it – this choice is for Arabian horses because of the closeness of their values; but for non-Arabian horses, he must assess without choice because of their variation. Zakat is not taken from the horse itself because the purpose of the poor is not achieved, since its flesh is not edible according to Abu Hanifa. According to him, it would be appropriate that zakat not be obligatory on horses because they are not edible, and their purpose is riding; therefore Allah the Exalted paired them with mules and donkeys, except that he left qiyas for the hadith, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'For every grazing horse, a dinar or ten dirhams.' And it is from his principle that qiyas is abandoned for a solitary hadith. His statement: 'Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: No zakat on horses,' and al-Shafi'i said the same.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the obligation of zakat on horses. It opens by contrasting horses with other grazing livestock (al-sawa'im) where zakat is due on males alone because they are edible. For horses, which are not edible according to Abu Hanifa, the issue is more complex. Two narrations are mentioned regarding females alone: the more correct view holds that zakat is obligatory because reproduction is possible through hired stallions. However, in al-Asl by Imam Muhammad, no zakat is due unless both males and females are present, as growth is through reproduction, not consumption. Consequently, the nisab is two (one male, one female) according to one narration, but another narration sets it at one male only. The author declares that the correct position requires mixing (ikhtilat). The core dispute is then stated: Abu Hanifa and Zufar obligate zakat on horses (for non-war purposes), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no zakat at all; for war horses, there is consensus of exemption. Abu Hanifa holds that zakat is paid from the value (qimah), not the horse itself, because the horse is not edible and does not benefit the poor directly. The owner has the choice to pay in value or to assess the horse's worth, but this choice applies only to Arabian horses whose values are similar; for non-Arabian horses, assessment is mandatory due to price disparities. The passage explains that Abu Hanifa's principle would suggest no zakat (qiyas: horses are like mules and donkeys, used for riding, not consumption), but he departs from qiyas based on a solitary hadith: 'For every grazing horse, a dinar or ten dirhams.' This reflects a foundational Hanafi usul principle that a solitary hadith (khabar wahid) can override qiyas. The passage concludes with the note that Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i reject zakat on horses altogether.

Key Terms
سائمة (sā'imah): Grazing livestock that spend most of the year in pasture; specifically here, horses that graze freely.
نصاب (niṣāb): The minimum threshold of wealth that triggers zakat; for horses, it is either one male horse (in one narration) or the value of 200 dirhams (implied).
اختلاط (ikhtilāṭ): Mixing of male and female horses; required according to the preferred narration for zakat to be due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Abu Hanifa's obligation of zakat on horses, despite his own qiyas suggesting otherwise?
A) The Quranic verse on zakat generally includes all livestock.
B) A mutawatir hadith specifying zakat on horses.
C) A solitary hadith that overrides qiyas in Hanafi usul.
D) Consensus (ijma) of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa obligates zakat on non-war grazing horses, departing from qiyas through a solitary hadith, a hallmark of Hanafi usul where weak texts can still override analogy.
