Zakat on Horses: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for horses with evidence and tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on zakat for horses, identify the evidence for each, and explain why the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.

Core Idea
Zakat on horses is not obligatory in the Hanafi school despite a dispute.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, regarding the obligation of zakat on horses. You will see how different hadith evidence and usul principles lead to conflicting rulings, and how the later fatwa settled on the Sahibayn's position. Understanding this dispute will sharpen your ability to weigh evidence and apply tarjih within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is the legal act being discussed? (Zakat on horses). Who disagrees and what are their positions?
Abu Hanifa: wajib. Sahibayn: no obligation. Shafi'i also agrees with Sahibayn.
2
State each position and its evidence
List the primary evidences: Abu Hanifa uses a weak hadith, Sahibayn use authentic hadiths that explicitly exempt horses.
Weak hadith: 'في كل فرس سائمة دينار'. Authentic: 'عفوت لكم عن صدقة الخيل' and 'ليس على المسلم في فرسه وعبده صدقة'.
3
Analyze the proof hierarchy and reasoning
Why did Abu Hanifa leave qiyas? (He left qiyas due to the weak hadith because in his usul, khabar al-wahid can override qiyas). Why did Sahibayn leave qiyas? (They left qiyas because the authentic hadiths are stronger).
The Sahibayn also considered horses edible, so qiyas would suggest zakat, but the explicit hadith overrides.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and tarjih basis
Which view is relied upon for fatwa? (Sahibayn). Why? (Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Kanz give fatwa on Sahibayn; al-Sarakhsi preferred Abu Hanifa but the majority of later scholars follow Sahibayn).
The text notes that even according to Abu Hanifa, the imam does not forcibly take zakat on horses.
5
Apply the practical consequence
What is the thamarah al-khilaf? In practice, no zakat is collected from horse owners, but voluntary payment is allowed.
This affects Muslim horse owners today in calculating their annual zakat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الخيل
وقالا : لا زكاة في الخيل .
الثانية مائتي درهم: أخذ بقدر ذلك، ولهذا قال: وإن شاء قومها.
قوله : فصاحبُها بالخيار احترز بهذا عن قول الطحاوي، فإنه يقول:
الخيار إلى العامل.
والأول هو الظاهر.
وقوله : وإن شاء قومها : هذا الخيار في أفراس العرب؛ لتقاربها في
القيمة، أما في أفراس العجم: فيقومها حتماً بغير خيار؛ لتفاوتها. وإنما لم تؤخذ زكاتها من عينها؛ لأن مقصود الفقراء لم يحصل به؛ لأن عينها غير مأكول عند أبي حنيفة.
وكان ينبغي عنده أن لا تجب الزكاة في الخيل؛ لأنها غير مأكولة عنده، وإنما المقصود منها الركوب، ولهذا قرنها الله تعالى بالبغال والحمير، إلا أنه تَرَكَ القياس فيها بالخبر، وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام:
في كل فرس سائمة دينار، أو عشرة دراهم
ومن أصله : أن القياس يترك بخبر الواحد.
قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا زكاة في الخيل)، وبه قال الشافعي
قال في فتاوى قاضي خان والفتوى على قولهما، وبه قطع في الكنز أيضاً.
وقال السرخسي: قول أبي حنيفة أولى.
قال في النهاية: وأجمعوا على أن الإمام لا يأخذ صدقة الخيل من صاحبها جبراً؛ لأن زكاتها لا تجب في عينها ، بخلاف زكاة السائمة، فإنها جزء من عينها، وللإمام فيه حق الأخذ.
ولأن الخيل مَطمَعُ لكل طامع ، فلو ولي السعاة أخذ الزكاة فيها: لم يتركوها لصاحبها.
وكان القياس عند أبي يوسف ومحمد : أن تجب الزكاة فيها؛ لأنها مأكولةٌ عندهما، وإنما تركا القياس لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «عفوتُ لكم عن صدقة الخيل والرقيق، إلا أن في الرقيق صدقة الفطر».
وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «ليس على المسلم في فرسه وعبده صدقة».
Chapter of Zakat on Horses
And they both said: There is no zakat on horses.
The second [category] is two hundred dirhams: he takes accordingly, and for this he said: And if he wishes, he may evaluate them.
His statement: The owner has the choice - by this he avoids the statement of al-Tahawi, for he says: The choice belongs to the agent.
And the first is apparent.
And his statement: And if he wishes, he may evaluate them: This choice is for Arabian horses, due to their closeness in value; as for non-Arabian horses, he evaluates them necessarily without choice due to their variation. And their zakat is not taken from the horses themselves because the purpose of the poor is not achieved thereby, because their flesh is not edible according to Abu Hanifa.
And according to him, it would be fitting that zakat not be obligatory on horses because they are not edible in his view, and their purpose is only riding, and for this Allah paired them with mules and donkeys, except that he left analogy in this due to the report, which is his statement, peace be upon him: 'For every grazing horse, a dinar or ten dirhams.'
And from his principle: analogy is abandoned due to a solitary report.
His statement: (And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: No zakat on horses), and al-Shafi'i said the same.
He said in Fatawa Qadikhan: And the fatwa is on their statement, and it is definitively stated in al-Kanz as well.
And al-Sarakhsi said: The statement of Abu Hanifa is more appropriate.
He said in al-Nihaya: And they have agreed that the imam does not forcibly take sadaqah of horses from their owner, because its zakat is not obligatory on the horses themselves, unlike zakat of grazing livestock, for it is part of the animals themselves, and the imam has the right to take it.
And because horses are coveted by every desirer, so if the collectors were to take zakat on them, they would not leave them for their owner.
And the analogy according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad was that zakat is obligatory on them, because they are edible in their view, but they abandoned analogy due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'I have waived for you sadaqah of horses and slaves, except that there is sadaqah of fitr on slaves.'
And he said, peace be upon him: 'There is no sadaqah upon a Muslim for his horse or his slave.'
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of zakat on horses. The matn states that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that there is no zakat on horses, while Abu Hanifa originally held that zakat is obligatory based on a weak hadith. The commentator explains that Abu Hanifa's position is based on the hadith 'for every grazing horse, a dinar or ten dirhams', which he considers sufficient to override qiyas (analogy) because in his usul, a weak hadith can override qiyas. However, the Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i rely on authentic hadiths that explicitly exempt horses and slaves from zakat. The mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa) is that of the Sahibayn, as recorded in Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Kanz. Additionally, the passage clarifies that even according to Abu Hanifa, the zakat is not taken from the horse itself but is evaluated in currency, and that the imam does not forcibly collect zakat on horses because it is not a direct part of the horse like in grazing livestock. The commentator also notes a secondary ruling: for Arabian horses, the owner may choose to evaluate or give from the horse, but for non-Arabian horses, evaluation is compulsory due to price variation.

Key Terms
سائمة (sā'ima): Grazing livestock left to pasture freely for most of the year.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāhid): A solitary hadith not reaching tawatur.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning comparing a new case to an established ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa) in the Hanafi school, is zakat obligatory on horses?
A) Yes, one dinar per horse.
B) Yes, but only for grazing horses.
C) No, no zakat on horses.
D) It depends on the horse's value.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative fatwa is that no zakat is due on horses, based on authentic hadiths and the tarjih of later jurists.

Zakat on Horses: The Mufta Bihi and the Qiyas Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on horse zakat with tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi position on zakat for horses and explain why Abu Hanifa differed using qiyas.

Core Idea
Horses are exempt from zakat by Prophetic hadith, overriding analogical reasoning.

The chapter on zakat of horses presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute. While Abu Hanifa initially held zakat obligatory by analogy to grazing livestock, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) relied on explicit hadith exempting horses. The fatwa today follows the Sahibayn. This lesson traces the arguments and the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the mufta bihi ruling: no zakat on horses. Note that this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
Also note that Abu Hanifa's view is not operative.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Abu Hanifa's view: zakat is obligatory by qiyas on grazing livestock because horses are eaten (in Hanafi school).
The basis is the illa of being eaten.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
The Sahibayn leave the qiyas due to the Prophet's hadith: 'I have exempted you from the sadaqah of horses and slaves.'
Also the hadith: 'There is no sadaqah on a Muslim regarding his horse and his slave.'
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's interpretation
Abu Hanifa restricts the exemption to riding horses, based on the mention of slaves (where fitrah is still due for service slaves).
He argues that the hadith does not cover horses for trade or breeding.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to the fatwa, no zakat is paid on horses. If one follows Abu Hanifa, zakat is due voluntarily but the imam cannot force collection.
The imam has no right to seize horses for zakat.

قال في فتاوى قاضي خان والفتوى على قولهما، وبه قطع في «الكنز» أيضاً.
وقال السرخسي: قول أبي حنيفة أولى.
قال في «النهاية»: وأجمعوا على أن الإمام لا يأخذ صدقة الخيل من صاحبها جبراً؛ لأن زكاتها لا تجب في عينها، بخلاف زكاة السائمة، فإنها جزء من عينها، وللإمام فيه حق الأخذ.
ولأن الخيل مَطمَعُ لكل طامع، فلو ولي السعاة أخذ الزكاة فيها: لم يتركوها لصاحبها.
وكان القياس عند أبي يوسف ومحمد: أن تجب الزكاة فيها؛ لأنها مأكولةٌ عندهما، وإنما تركا القياس لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «عفوت لكم عن صدقة الخيل والرقيق، إلا أن في الرقيق صدقة الفطر».
وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «ليس على المسلم في فرسه وعبده صدقة».
ولا شيء في البغال والحمير، إلا أن تكون للتجارة.
وليس في الفُصْلانِ والحُمْلانِ، والعَجَاجيـل صدقة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، إلا أن يكون معها كبار.
إلا أن أبا حنيفة يَحملُ ما روياه على فرس الركوب، بدليل قوله: «والرقيق، إلا أن في الرقيق صدقة الفطر»، والفطرة إنما تجب في عبد الخدمة.
قوله: (ولا شيء في البغال والحمير، إلا أن تكون للتجارة). لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «ليس في الكسعة شيء». وهي الحمير، والبغال ملحقة بها.
وقوله إلا أن تكون للتجارة؛ لأن الزكاة حينئذ تتعلق بالمالية، كسائر أموال التجارة.
قوله: (وليس في الفصلان، والعجاجيل، والحملان صدقة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، إلا أن يكون فيها كبار).
الفصلان: جمع فصيل، وهو أولاد الإبل.
والحملان بضم الحاء وكسرها جمع الحمل، وهم أولاد الغنم.
والعجاجيل: أولاد البقر.
Chapter of Zakat of Horses. He said in Fatawa Qadikhan: The fatwa is on their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) statement, and it is definitively stated in al-Kanz also. Al-Sarakhsi said: Abu Hanifa's statement is more appropriate. He said in al-Nihayah: They unanimously agreed that the Imam does not take the sadaqah of horses from their owner forcibly, because its zakat is not obligatory on the substance itself, unlike the zakat of grazing livestock, for it is a part of the substance, and the Imam has a right to take it. And because the horse is an object of desire for every covetous person, so if the collectors were to take zakat on them, they would not leave them for their owner. The analogy according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad was that zakat should be obligatory on them, because they are eaten according to them, but they left the analogy due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'I have exempted you from the sadaqah of horses and slaves, except that there is zakat al-fitr on slaves.' And he said (peace be upon him): 'There is no sadaqah on a Muslim regarding his horse and his slave.' And there is nothing on mules and donkeys, unless they are for trade. And there is no sadaqah on young camels (fuslan), lambs (humlan), and calves (ajajil) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, unless there are mature ones with them. Except that Abu Hanifa interprets what they narrated as referring to the riding horse, by the evidence of his saying: 'and slaves, except that there is zakat al-fitr on slaves,' and fitrah is only obligatory on a slave used for service. His statement: 'And there is nothing on mules and donkeys, unless they are for trade.' Due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'There is nothing on the kas'a' - which are donkeys, and mules are annexed to them. And his statement 'unless they are for trade' because zakat then attaches to the monetary value, like other trade goods. His statement: 'And there is no sadaqah on fuslan, ajajil, and humlan according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, unless there are mature ones among them.' Fuslan is plural of fasil, which are young camels. Humlan (with damm or kasr of ha) is plural of hamal, which are young sheep. Ajajil are young cattle.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling statement regarding zakat on horses, mules, donkeys, and young livestock. The chapter begins with the issue of zakat on horses. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to Qadikhan is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), who hold that zakat is not obligatory on horses because of the Prophet's (peace be upon him) hadith: 'I have exempted you from the sadaqah of horses and slaves...' and 'There is no sadaqah on a Muslim regarding his horse and his slave.' Abu Hanifa originally held that zakat is obligatory by analogy because horses are eaten (according to Hanafi view) and thus similar to grazing livestock, but he abandoned the analogy due to the prophetic decree. However, Abu Hanifa interprets the exemption as applying only to riding horses, not to horses kept for trade or breeding. The commentary clarifies that even though the Sahibayn's view is the fatwa, some later scholars like Sarakhsi prefer Abu Hanifa's position. The passage then states that mules and donkeys are exempt from zakat unless they are traded, based on a hadith ('there is nothing on the kas'a' – meaning donkeys). Finally, young camels (fuslan), lambs (humlan), and calves (ajajil) are not subject to zakat according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad unless there are mature animals with them, because the zakat on livestock is on the mature animals, not the young when they are separate. The commentary defines the terms for young livestock.

Key Terms
زكاة الخيل (zakāt al-khayl): Zakat on horses; the chapter discusses whether it is obligatory.
السعاة (al-suʿāt): Zakat collectors appointed by the imam.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; here, Abu Hanifa analogizes horses to grazing livestock.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what is the ruling on zakat for horses?
A) Zakat is obligatory on all horses.
B) Zakat is not obligatory on any horses.
C) Zakat is obligatory only on riding horses.
D) Zakat is obligatory on horses used for trade.

Key Takeaway
Horses are exempt from zakat by explicit hadith, and the relied-upon Hanafi position is that no zakat is due, even though Abu Hanifa differed.

Zakat on Mules and Donkeys: Exemption and the Exception for Trade
Ruling statement on mules and donkeys with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on zakat for mules and donkeys and explain the exception for trade goods.

Core Idea
Mules and donkeys are exempt from zakat unless they are trade goods.

The passage continues by addressing mules and donkeys. The ruling is clear: no zakat on these animals unless they are used for trade. This is based on a hadith about 'al-kas'a', meaning donkeys, and mules are analogized to them. This lesson clarifies that ruling and the trade exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State that there is no zakat on mules and donkeys in principle.
This is the default ruling.
2
Find the dalil
The hadith: 'ليس في الكسعة شيء' — there is no sadaqah on donkeys. Mules are included by analogy.
'Al-kas'a' is a term for donkeys.
3
Note the exception
If the animals are for trade, zakat applies because it attaches to monetary value like other trade goods.
This is similar to horses: trade triggers zakat.
4
Apply the ruling
For a person with mules or donkeys, determine if they are for personal use or trade.
If for riding or work, no zakat; if bought and sold as merchandise, zakat is due.

Al-Sarakhsi said: Abu Hanifa's statement is more appropriate. And he said (peace be upon him): 'There is no sadaqah on a Muslim regarding his horse and his slave.' And there is nothing on mules and donkeys, unless they are for trade. And there is no sadaqah on young camels (fuslan), lambs (humlan), and calves (ajajil) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, unless there are mature ones with them. Except that Abu Hanifa interprets what they narrated as referring to the riding horse, by the evidence of his saying: 'and slaves, except that there is zakat al-fitr on slaves,' and fitrah is only obligatory on a slave used for service. His statement: 'And there is nothing on mules and donkeys, unless they are for trade.' Due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'There is nothing on the kas'a' - which are donkeys, and mules are annexed to them. And his statement 'unless they are for trade' because zakat then attaches to the monetary value, like other trade goods. His statement: 'And there is no sadaqah on fuslan, ajajil, and humlan according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, unless there are mature ones among them.' Fuslan is plural of fasil, which are young camels. Humlan (with damm or kasr of ha) is plural of hamal, which are young sheep. Ajajil are young cattle.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling statement regarding zakat on horses, mules, donkeys, and young livestock. The chapter begins with the issue of zakat on horses. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to Qadikhan is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), who hold that zakat is not obligatory on horses because of the Prophet's (peace be upon him) hadith: 'I have exempted you from the sadaqah of horses and slaves...' and 'There is no sadaqah on a Muslim regarding his horse and his slave.' Abu Hanifa originally held that zakat is obligatory by analogy because horses are eaten (according to Hanafi view) and thus similar to grazing livestock, but he abandoned the analogy due to the prophetic decree. However, Abu Hanifa interprets the exemption as applying only to riding horses, not to horses kept for trade or breeding. The commentary clarifies that even though the Sahibayn's view is the fatwa, some later scholars like Sarakhsi prefer Abu Hanifa's position. The passage then states that mules and donkeys are exempt from zakat unless they are traded, based on a hadith ('there is nothing on the kas'a' – meaning donkeys). Finally, young camels (fuslan), lambs (humlan), and calves (ajajil) are not subject to zakat according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad unless there are mature animals with them, because the zakat on livestock is on the mature animals, not the young when they are separate. The commentary defines the terms for young livestock.

Key Terms
البغال (al-bighāl): Mules; offspring of a male donkey and a female horse.
الحمير (al-ḥamīr): Donkeys.
الكسعة (al-kasʿa): Term used in hadith for donkeys.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for exempting mules from zakat?
A) The same hadith that exempts horses.
B) A hadith about donkeys, and mules are annexed.
C) Consensus that mules are not livestock.
D) Qiyas on camels.

Key Takeaway
Mules and donkeys are exempt from zakat unless they are trade goods, based on a specific hadith.

Zakat on Young Livestock: When Young Animals Are Not Zakatable
Ruling statement on young livestock exemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that young camels, sheep, and cattle are not subject to zakat unless accompanied by mature animals.

Core Idea
Young livestock are exempt from zakat when they are separate from mature animals.

The passage concludes with a ruling on young livestock. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, young camels (fuslan), lambs (humlan), and calves (ajajil) are not zakatable unless there are mature animals with them. This lesson clarifies that the zakat is on the mature animals, and the young are not counted separately unless they form a herd with adults.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animals
List the young animals: fuslan (young camels), humlan (lambs), ajajil (calves).
These are the offspring of zakatable livestock.
2
State the ruling
There is no zakat on these young animals according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Abu Yusuf's view is not mentioned; assume agreement or silence.
3
Note the exception
If there are mature animals with the young, then zakat is due on the whole herd.
The young then count towards the total when combined with adults.
4
Understand the rationale
Zakat on livestock is on the animals that have reached the nisab; young alone do not constitute a nisab unless they reach the age of maturity or are accompanied by adults.
This prevents fragmentation of herds to avoid zakat.

No Zakat on Mules and Donkeys Unless for Trade
Ruling on zakat for mules and donkeys with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for mules and donkeys, identify the hadith proof, and apply the exception for trade goods.

Core Idea
Mules and donkeys are zakat-free unless used for trade; the hadith 'لا شيء في الكسعة' is the proof.

This lesson addresses a common question: do mules and donkeys require zakat like camels and goats? The Hanafi school, relying on a clear hadith, rules that no zakat is due on these animals unless they are kept for trade. The exception aligns with general rules of trade zakat.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the default ruling: no zakat is due on mules and donkeys.
This is the position of the Hanafi school and is agreed upon.
2
Locate the hadith proof
The author cites the hadith 'ليس في الكسعة شيء' (There is nothing on al-kas'ah). Understand that 'al-kas'ah' refers to young donkeys, and mules are annexed by analogy.
The hadith is ahad but clear in meaning.
3
Identify the exception
When mules and donkeys are kept for trade, zakat becomes due on their monetary value, like other trade goods.
The condition is they must be trade inventory, not just for personal use.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a farmer who owns a mule for plowing: no zakat. But if he buys mules to resell, zakat is due on their value if it reaches nisab.
The key is intention and usage.

And there is nothing (no zakat) on mules and donkeys, unless they are for trade. His statement: (And there is nothing on mules and donkeys, unless they are for trade). Because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'There is nothing on al-kas'ah.' And they are the donkeys, and mules are annexed to them. And his statement 'unless they are for trade' because the zakat then attaches to the monetary value, like all trade goods.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states the Hanafi position that no zakat is due on mules and donkeys unless they are kept for trade. The proof is a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ: 'There is nothing on al-kas'ah' which refers to the young of donkeys. Mules are annexed to donkeys by analogy since they are also non-zakatable animals. If they are trade goods, zakat becomes due on their value because the basis of zakat is the monetary value, similar to other trade merchandise. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is agreed upon by the schools.

Key Terms
البغال (al-bighāl): Mules, the offspring of a horse and a donkey.
الكسعة (al-kas'ah): Young of donkeys, as mentioned in the hadith.

Zakat on Young Livestock: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for foals, lambs, calves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three consecutive positions of Abu Hanifa on zakat for young livestock, compare the views of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
No zakat on young livestock unless with adults—the mature animal is the basis.

This passage presents a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for young livestock animals. Imam Abu Hanifa himself changed his position three times, and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, each adopted a different stage. Understanding this evolution reveals how Hanafi fiqh develops through talil (reasoning) and tarjih (preference).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
List Abu Hanifa's three successive positions: (1) zakat like adults, (2) one animal due, (3) no zakat. Note which disciple adopted each.
Muhammad adopted the final position; Abu Yusuf adopted the second; Zufar kept the first.
2
Analyze the dalil of the final position
Abu Hanifa analogizes young livestock to a service slave: just as zakat al-fitr is not due on a slave who cannot serve, young animals not yet productive are not zakatable.
This is a qiyas with illa of lack of productivity.
3
Examine the opposing views
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that one young animal is due; Zufar and Malik hold that adult zakat applies. Understand their basis: young are wealth and should be subject to zakat like adults.
The dispute hinges on analogy versus direct wealth consideration.
4
Apply the nisab completion rule
If one adult exists among young, the herd completes the nisab for zakat, but zakat must be paid from the adult, not the young.
This shows the distinction between nisab completion and zakat payment.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the final view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no zakat on young alone.
This is the position in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس في الفُصْلانِ والحُمْلانِ ، والعَجَاجيـل صـدقة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، إلا أن يكون معها كبار. إلا أن أبا حنيفة يَحملُ ما روياه على فرس الركوب، بدليل قوله: «والرقيق، إلا أن في الرقيق صدقة الفطر»، والفطرة إنما تجب في عبد الخدمة. قوله: (وليس في الفصلان، والعجاجيل، والحملان صدقة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، إلا أن يكون فيها كبار). الفُصلان: جمع: فَصيل، وهو أولاد الإبل. والحملان بضم الحاء وكسرها جمع: الحمل، وهم أولاد الغنم. والعجاجيل: أولاد البقر. وقال أبو يوسف : تجب فيها واحدة منها . قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف: فيها واحدة منها). وقال زفر : فيها ما في الكبار، وبه قال مالك. وكان أبو حنيفة أولاً يقول: يجب فيها ما يجب في الكبار، وبه أخذ زفر ومالك، ثم رجع فقال: تجب فيها واحدة منها، وبه أخذ أبو يوسف، والشافعي، ثم رجع ، وقال : لا يجب فيها شيء، وبه أخذ محمد. وأما إذا كان فيها واحدة من المُسنَّات: جعل الكل تبعاً لها في انعقادها نصاباً، دون تأدية الزكاة، حتى لا يجزيه أخذ واحدة من الصغار. وصورة المسألة: إذا اشترى خمسةً وعشرين فصيلاً، أو أربعين حَمَلاً، أو ثلاثين عجلاً، أو وهب له ذلك : هل ينعقد عليها الحول؟ فعند أبي حنيفة ومحمد : لا، وعند أبي يوسف: ينعقد، حتى لو حال الحول من حين ملكه : تجب الزكاة. وصورة أخرى : إذا كان له نصاب سائمة، فحال عليها ستة أشهر،
And there is no zakat on foals, lambs, and calves according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, unless there are adults with them. Except that Abu Hanifa carries what they narrated to the riding horse, based on his statement: 'And slaves, except that on slaves there is zakat al-fitr,' and fitrah is only obligatory on a service slave. His statement: (And there is no charity on foals, calves, and lambs according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, unless there are adults among them). Al-fuslan: plural of fasil, which is the young of camels. Al-humlan, with damma or kasra on the ha, plural of hamal, which are the young of sheep. Al-‘ajajil: young of cows. And Abu Yusuf said: One (zakat payment) is due from them. His statement: (And Abu Yusuf said: one from them). And Zufar said: what is due from adults is due from them, and Malik said the same. Abu Hanifa initially said: what is due from adults is due from them, and Zufar and Malik adopted this; then he retracted and said: one is due from them, and Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi‘i adopted this; then he retracted and said: nothing is due from them, and Muhammad adopted this. And if there is one adult among them, they are all made subservient to it in completing the nisab, but not for paying zakat, so that it is not permissible to take one from the young. A case: if one buys twenty-five foals, or forty lambs, or thirty calves, or is gifted them: does the year begin? According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no; according to Abu Yusuf: yes, and if a year passes from possession, zakat is due. Another case: if he has a nisab of grazing livestock, and six months have passed...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning zakat on young livestock animals (foals, lambs, calves). The initial position of Abu Hanifa and his later position with Muhammad is that no zakat is due on young animals unless they are accompanied by adults. Abu Hanifa analogizes this to the ruling on slaves: just as zakat al-fitr is only obligatory on a slave fit for service, similarly, young animals are not zakatable. Abu Yusuf opines that one animal (from the young) is due. Zufar and Malik say that the zakat due is the same as for adults. The text also presents a case where a single adult among young completes the nisab for the whole group, but the zakat payment must come from the adult, not the young. This shows the nuance of the Hanafi school on livestock zakat. The mufta bihi position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no zakat on young animals unless mixed with adults.

Key Terms
الفُصْلان (al-fuṣlān): Young camels, plural of fasil.
الحُمْلان (al-ḥumlān): Young sheep, plural of hamal.
العَجَاجيل (al-‘ajājīl): Young cows, calves.
المُسَنَّات (al-musannāt): Mature/old animals, singular musinnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat is due as if they were adults
B) One animal is due
C) No zakat is due
D) Zakat is due only if the young are with an adult

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi is that no zakat is due on young livestock unless an adult is present, based on the analogy of a non-productive slave.

Zakat on Horses: Abu Hanifa's Three Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat of horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's three positions on zakat of horses and identify which is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's final position — no zakat on horses — is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira examines the intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat of horses. Abu Hanifa held three different views over his scholarly career, and the passage traces his development. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial because it shows how the final mufta bihi position emerged, and it clarifies that horses are not subject to zakat in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its disputed ruling
What is the act under discussion? Zakat on horses. Which ruling is disputed? Whether zakat is obligatory, and if so, how much.
2
Follow the three positions of Abu Hanifa
The passage mentions three chronological stages: first, zakat like cattle; second, one horse; third, nothing. List each.
The first position is attributed to Zufar and Malik; the second to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i; the third to Muhammad.
3
Compare the positions of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf holds the second view (one horse); Muhammad holds the third view (nothing). Zufar holds the first view.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The final view of Abu Hanifa (no zakat) adopted by Muhammad becomes the relied-upon position. Reasons: it is the settled view after retractions and is transmitted in Zahir al-Riwayah.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and later authorities adopt this.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under the mufta bihi, horse owners pay no zakat. Under Abu Yusuf's view, they would give one horse. Under Zufar's view, zakat like cattle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الخيل
وقال أبو يوسف : تجب فيها واحدة منها .
فإن قيل: ليست هذه المسألة من جنس الخيل، فلم أوردها فيها؟
قيل: لأن زكاة الخيل مختلف فيها، والزكاة في هذه الأشياء مختلف
فيها أيضاً، فأوردها فيها.
* قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف: فيها واحدة منها).
وقال زفر : فيها ما في الكبار، وبه قال مالك(١).
وكان أبو حنيفة أولاً يقول: يجب فيها ما يجب في الكبار، وبه أخذ زفر ومالك، ثم رجع فقال: تجب فيها واحدة منها، وبه أخذ أبو يوسف،
والشافعي، ثم رجع ، وقال : لا يجب فيها شيء، وبه أخذ محمد.
Chapter on Zakat of Horses. Abu Yusuf said: Zakat is obligatory in it as one (horse). If it is said: This issue is not of the same kind as horses, so why did you include it here? It is said: Because zakat on horses is disputed, and zakat on these things is also disputed, so I included it here. His statement: (And Abu Yusuf said: In it, one [horse]). Zufar said: In it is what is in the large (cattle), and Malik held the same. Abu Hanifa initially used to say: What is obligatory in the large (cattle) is obligatory in it, and Zufar and Malik adopted that. Then he retracted and said: One horse is obligatory in it, and Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i adopted that. Then he retracted and said: Nothing is obligatory in it, and Muhammad adopted that.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat of horses. The commentator begins by citing Abu Yusuf's view that one horse must be given as zakat. He then explains why the issue is included here despite being different from cattle: because both involve disputed zakat rulings. The passage then details Abu Hanifa's three positions: initially he equated horses to cattle (supporting Zufar and Malik), then he changed to requiring one horse (supported by Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i), and finally he retracted to no zakat at all (supported by Muhammad). Thus Abu Hanifa's final position is that no zakat is due on horses, which is the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
زكاة الخيل (zakāt al-khayl): The legal ruling on whether zakat is due on horses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat is due like cattle
B) One horse is due
C) No zakat is due
D) Zakat is due only on working horses

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's final position, adopted by Imam Muhammad, is that no zakat is due on horses, and this is the operative ruling (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Hawl for Young Livestock and Offspring
Intra-Hanafi dispute on two sub-issues of zakat hawl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf on whether the hawl begins for purchased/gifted young livestock and whether the hawl of mothers transfers to offspring, and identify the mufta bihi opinion.

Core Idea
Young livestock do not start a zakat hawl until maturity; the hawl of dams does not pass to offspring.

Zakat on livestock depends on the passage of a full lunar year (hawl). But what if the animals are young—do they begin to accumulate hawl immediately? And if a mother dies, does her hawl transfer to her offspring? The Hanafi school has an internal difference of opinion on these two questions. This lesson walks through the arguments and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sub-issues
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: (1) someone owns young camels/sheep/cattle either by purchase or gift—does the hawl begin from the time of ownership? (2) A nisab of grazing livestock gives birth, then the mothers die—do the offspring inherit the hawl?
These are separate rulings with a common underlying principle.
2
State the positions
For both issues, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say 'no' (the hawl does not begin for the young, and does not transfer). Abu Yusuf says 'yes' in both cases.
The text explicitly contrasts 'عندهما لا' with 'قال أبو يوسف: يبقى'.
3
Identify the underlying reasoning
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad treat young animals as not yet subject to zakat until they reach the age of maturity; thus they cannot commence a hawl. Similarly, the offspring are a new generation, separate from their mothers. Abu Yusuf considers the group of young animals as a potential nisab that starts the hawl immediately, and the offspring as a continuation of the mothers' nisab.
The text does not give the reasoning explicitly, but it is inferred from the rulings.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The phrase 'فعندهما لا' indicates that the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold the negative view, and this is the relied-upon position (zahir riwayah).
The later Hanafi tradition follows the majority opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كان فيها واحدة من المُسنَّات: جعل الكل تبعاً لها في انعقادها نصاباً، دون تأدية الزكاة، حتى لا يجزيه أخذ واحدة من الصغار. وصورة المسألة: إذا اشترى خمسةً وعشرين فصيلاً، أو أربعين حَمَلاً، أو ثلاثين عجلاً، أو وهب له ذلك : هل ينعقد عليها الحول؟ فعند أبي حنيفة ومحمد : لا، وعند أبي يوسف: ينعقد، حتى لو حال الحول من حين ملكه : تجب الزكاة. وصورة أخرى : إذا كان له نصاب سائمة، فحال عليها ستة أشهر، فتوالدت مثل عددها، ثم هلكت الأصول وبقيت الأولاد: هل يبقى حول الأصول على الأولاد؟ فعندهما لا، وقال: أبو يوسف : يبقى.
And as for when there is one among the mature animals: the whole is made dependent on it for the completion of the nisab, without paying zakat from it, so that it is not permissible for him to take one of the young. And the form of the issue: if he purchased twenty‑five young camels, or forty lambs, or thirty calves, or they were gifted to him: does the hawl begin? According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no; according to Abu Yusuf: yes, such that if a year passes from the time he owned them, zakat becomes due. And another form: if he has a nisab of grazing livestock, and six months passed, and they gave birth to the like of their number, then the mothers perished and the offspring remained: does the hawl of the mothers remain on the offspring? According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): no; and Abu Yusuf said: it remains.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage addresses two sub‑issues regarding zakat on livestock. First, when a person possesses a group of young animals together with one mature animal, the mature animal completes the nisab, making the entire group a nisab for zakat purposes. However, zakat is not to be taken from the young; the owner must pay from the mature category. Then two scenarios are presented: (1) If one buys or receives as a gift a certain number of young animals (e.g., 25 young camels), does the hawl (year) begin from the time of ownership? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say no; Abu Yusuf says yes. (2) If one already had a nisab of grazing livestock, and after six months they give birth to as many as themselves, then the mothers die, do the offspring inherit the hawl of the mothers? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say no; Abu Yusuf says yes. The practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the hawl for young animals starts when they become mature, and offspring start a new hawl.

Key Terms
الحول (al-ḥawl): The one-year lunar period that must pass for zakat to become obligatory on the nisab.
السائمة (al-sā'imah): Grazing livestock that feed themselves from the land for most of the year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When they become mature
C) After one year from purchase
D) Never, because they are not a nisab

Key Takeaway
For livestock zakat, the hawl for young animals begins only at maturity, and the hawl of mothers does not transfer to their offspring.

Choice of Sadiq or Owner When Specific Age Unavailable
Intra-Hanafi dispute on collector vs. owner choice for alternative zakat animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of al-Isbijabi and al-Sayrafi regarding who chooses the alternative zakat animal, and apply the correct rule based on the payment method.

Core Idea
When the exact zakat age is missing, who chooses depends on whether payment is by purchase or value.

This passage addresses a practical problem in zakat collection: what happens when a person owes a specific age of livestock but does not own that exact age? The zakat collector can take a higher age and refund the excess, or take a lower age and collect the difference. The matn appears to give the choice to the collector, but the commentary introduces a nuanced dispute that ultimately turns on the nature of the payment.

Guided Path
1
State the basic scenario
Identify that a person owes a specific age of zakat animal but does not have it, and that two alternatives exist: the collector takes a higher age and refunds, or takes a lower age and collects the deficit.
2
Identify the two positions
Al-Isbijabi holds that the collector chooses unconditionally, based on the matn's apparent meaning. The commentator states that the correct view is that the owner chooses.
The commentator says 'al-Sawab' (the correct view) is owner's choice.
3
Introduce al-Sayrafi's differentiation
Al-Sayrafi refines: when the alternative involves paying extra (collector pays refund), it is a purchase, so the collector chooses. When the alternative involves paying a deficit (owner pays compensation), it is a payment by value, so the owner chooses by consensus.
4
Apply the rule to the specific cases
If the owner wants to pay part of a higher age animal (tashqis), the collector chooses due to harm in division. The compensation between consecutive ages is two sheep or twenty dirhams.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view for fatwa is al-Sayrafi's differentiation, as favored by later Hanafi authorities and the commentator.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن وجب عليه سن، فلم يوجد معه: أخذ المصدق أعلى منها، ورد الفضل، أو أخذ دونَها وأَخَذَ الفضل).
ظاهر هذا : أن الخيار إلى المصدق، وهو قول الإسبيجابي.
والصواب أن الخيار إلى صاحب المال.

بالقيمة، وفي دفع القيمة الخيار إلى صاحب المال، بالإجماع. فإن وجب بنت لبون، وأراد أن يدفع بعض حقة: فالخيار إلى المصدق ؛ لما في التشقيص من الضرر.
والتفاوت بين بنت المخاض وبنت اللبون شاتان، أو عشرون درهماً.

His statement: (And whoever owes a specific [age of animal], and it is not found with him: the collector takes a higher [age] and returns the excess, or takes a lower [age] and takes the excess). The apparent [meaning] of this is that the choice is for the collector, and that is the saying of al-Isbijabi. And the correct [view] is that the choice is for the owner. Al-Sayrafi said: The correct [view] is that the choice is for the collector when it involves paying extra, because in the amount of the extra, it is a purchase. And [the choice is] for the owner if he wants to pay the lower and the excess, because it is payment by value, and in payment by value, the choice is for the owner, by consensus. If a bint labun is due, and he wants to pay part of a hiqqah: then the choice is for the collector, because of the harm in division. And the difference between bint makhad and bint labun is two sheep or twenty dirhams. And between bint labun and hiqqah: likewise. And between hiqqah and jadha'ah: likewise.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where a person owes a specific age of animal for zakat (e.g., a two‑year‑old cow) but does not have that exact age. Two options exist: the zakat collector can take a higher age and refund the difference, or take a lower age and collect the difference. The question is who chooses between these options. The apparent implication of the matn is that the collector chooses, and al‑Isbijabi holds this view. However, the commentator states that the correct view is that the owner chooses. Then al‑Sayrafi refines: if paying a higher age (collector pays extra), the collector chooses because it is a purchase for the excess; if paying a lower age (owner pays the deficit), the owner chooses because it is a payment of value, where the owner has choice by consensus. An exception: if the owner wants to pay part of a higher‑age animal (e.g., a share in a hiqqah), the collector chooses due to potential harm in dividing the animal. The passage also lists the standard compensation differences between consecutive ages: two sheep or twenty dirhams each.

Key Terms
المصدق (al-muṣaddiq): The official zakat collector appointed by the state.
التشقيص (al-tashqīṣ): Dividing an animal into shares (partial ownership).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The collector has the choice
C) Both must agree
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
When the exact zakat age is unavailable, the choice between collector and owner depends on whether the alternative involves a purchase (collector chooses) or a value payment (owner chooses), per al-Sayrafi's accepted differentiation.

The Haul of Offspring When the Originals Perish
Intra-Hanafi dispute on haul continuation for offspring

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether the haul of original zakatable animals continues for their offspring after the originals die, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
The haul is tied to the original animals; when they die, the offspring start a new haul.

Zakat on livestock requires a full lunar year (hawl) of ownership. But what happens if the original animals die and only their offspring remain? Does the haul transfer? This short passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira presents a clear intra-Hanafi dispute: the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the haul does not continue, while Abu Yusuf argues it does. Understanding the basis of this difference sharpens your grasp of zakat conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the Arabic: the original animals (usul) give birth to the same number of offspring, then the originals perish, leaving only the offspring. The question is whether the haul of the originals continues upon the offspring.
Note that both groups are zakatable animals (sa'imah).
2
State the Majority Hanafi Position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ('the two'), the haul does not continue. The offspring begin a new haul from the date the originals died (or from when they became independent property).
This is indicated by 'عندهما لا' in the text.
3
Identify the Opposing View
Abu Yusuf holds that the haul continues. His reasoning: the offspring are a direct replacement for the originals and share the same wealth status.
The text says 'وقال أبو يوسف يبقى'.
4
Analyse the Basis of Disagreement
The dispute hinges on whether offspring are considered separate property or a continuation of the original animals. The majority view treats each individual animal as a separate property unit for the haul condition.
This is a pure Hanafi internal difference in usul (methodology).
5
Determine the Practical Impact
If the haul does not continue, zakat on the offspring may be delayed until they complete their own haul. If it continues, zakat becomes due at the original haul's completion date.
This difference can affect whether zakat is due in a given year.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتوالدت مثل عددها، ثم هلكت الأصول وبقيت الأولاد: هل يبقى حول الأصول على الأولاد؟
فعندهما لا، وقال: أبو يوسف : يبقى.
Then they gave birth to the same number [of offspring], then the original [animals] perished and the offspring remained: does the haul of the originals continue upon the offspring?
Thus according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad]: no, and Abu Yusuf said: it continues.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the zakat haul (year) for livestock. The scenario: an owner has a certain number of zakatable animals, they give birth to the same number of offspring, then the original animals die, leaving only the offspring. The question is whether the haul (the one-year holding period) of the original animals transfers to the offspring for the purpose of completing the zakat obligation. According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the haul does not continue; the offspring start a new haul. However, Abu Yusuf holds that the haul does continue because the offspring are a replacement for the originals in terms of property.

Key Terms
حول (hawl): The lunar year that must pass for zakat to become due on livestock and other wealth.
أصول (usul): The original animals owned that are subject to zakat.
أولاد (awlād): Offspring (young) of the zakatable animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The haul does not continue; offspring start a new haul
C) The haul is halved
D) Zakat becomes due immediately

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the haul does not transfer to offspring; they begin a new haul, ensuring the condition is strictly met.

The Option for Collector vs Owner in Zakat Age Substitution
Explains the rule of taking higher or lower age and the dispute over who has the choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on who has the option when the prescribed age for zakat is unavailable and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
The option in zakat age substitution depends on whether the extra is a purchase or cash payment.

When a zakat payer does not own the exact required age of animal, the collector may take a higher or lower age with compensation. But who decides? The Hanafi school has different views within it, and the correct ruling depends on the nature of the transaction. This lesson unpacks the dispute between al-Isbijabi, al-Sayrafi, and the author of the matn.

Guided Path
1
State the apparent ruling from the matn
The base text says the collector takes a higher or lower age and returns or collects the difference. This implies the collector has the option.
This is the starting point that triggers the dispute.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi opposition
Al-Isbijabi holds that the collector always has the option. The author then says the correct view is that the owner has the option. Al-Sayrafi further differentiates.
Note that the opposition is within the Hanafi school, not between schools.
3
Analyze al-Sayrafi's differentiation
Al-Sayrafi distinguishes: if the owner gives a higher age (paying extra), it is a purchase, so the collector has the option. If the owner gives a lower age and pays cash, it is payment by value, so the owner has the option.
The key principle: the nature of the compensation (in-kind vs cash) determines the option.
4
Examine the exception for partitioning
If the owner wants to give part of a different age (e.g., part of a hiqqah for a bint labun), the collector has the option due to potential harm in partitioning.
This shows the school's concern for preventing harm (darar).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical effect: if the option is with the collector, the owner may be forced to give a higher age even if he prefers cash. If with the owner, the owner can choose the method that suits him.
Understanding this helps in actual zakat collection and payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (ومن وجب عليه سن، فلم يوجد معه: أخذ المصدق أعلى منها، ورد الفضل، أو أخذ دونَها وأَخَذَ الفضل).
ظاهر هذا : أن الخيار إلى المصدق، وهو قول الإسبيجابي.
والصواب أن الخيار إلى صاحب المال.
:
قال الصيرفي : الصحيح أن الخيار إلى المصدق: إذا كان فيه دفع زيادة؛ لأنه في مقدار الزيادة: شراء.
وإلى صاحب المال إذا أراد أن يدفع الأدنى والزيادة؛ لأنه دفع
بالقيمة، وفي دفع القيمة الخيار إلى صاحب المال، بالإجماع. فإن وجب بنت لبون، وأراد أن يدفع بعض حقة: فالخيار إلى المصدق ؛ لما في التشقيص من الضرر.

* His statement: (And whoever owes a [certain] age, and it is not found with him: the collector takes a higher [age] from him, and returns the surplus, or takes a lower [age] and takes the surplus.
The apparent [meaning] of this is that the choice is with the collector, and that is the statement of al-Isbijabi.
And the correct [view] is that the choice is with the owner of the wealth.
:
Al-Sayrafi said: The correct [view] is that the choice is with the collector: when there is payment of an increase; because in the amount of the increase it is a purchase.
And [the choice is] with the owner of the wealth when he wants to pay the lower and the increase; because he paid by value, and in payment by value the choice is with the owner of the wealth, by consensus. If a bint labun is due, and he wants to pay part of a hiqqah: then the choice is with the collector; due to the harm in partitioning.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about who has the option when the exact prescribed age for zakat on cattle is not available. The matn states that the collector may take a higher age and return the excess or take a lower age and collect the deficit. The commentator explains that the apparent view of al-Isbijabi is that the option is with the collector. However, the correct view according to the author is that the option is with the owner. Al-Sayrafi then provides a nuanced position: if the owner wants to pay a higher age (and thus pay extra), it is considered a purchase, so the collector has the choice to accept because it is a new transaction; but if the owner wants to pay a lower age and pay the deficit in cash, then the option is with the owner because paying by value gives the owner the choice. If the owner wants to pay part of a different age (e.g., part of a hiqqah for a bint labun), then the collector has the choice because partitioning causes harm. The passage then lists the standard differences in value between the various ages of camels, which are used for calculating the compensation.

Key Terms
سن (sinn): The prescribed age of the animal for zakat, e.g., bint makhad, bint labun, etc.
مصدق (musaddiq): The zakat collector appointed by the state or imam to collect zakat.
خيار (khiyar): The right of choice or option to decide whether to take the higher or lower age with compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The option in zakat age substitution is determined by whether the compensation is paid in kind (collector's option) or in cash (owner's option).

Paying Value in Zakat and Other Obligations: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on paying monetary value in various obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on paying qimah in zakat, nadhr, kaffarat, ushr, fitr, hady, and udhiyah, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Hanafis allow cash value in charity obligations but not in ritual sacrifices – purpose determines permissibility.

In many acts of worship, the law prescribes a specific item—e.g., a sheep for zakat or a goat for udhiyah. But can you give its cash value instead? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools answer this differently. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi stance and the underlying rationale, focusing on the distinction between pure financial obligations and ritual acts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the five permissible obligations
Identify the obligations where Hanafis allow paying value: zakat, nadhr (vows), kaffarat (expiation), ushr (tithe), and sadaqat al-fitr (zakat al-fitr).
These are obligations whose primary purpose is to meet the need of the poor or to compensate for a shortcoming.
2
State the Hanafi prohibition for hady and udhiyah
Note that paying value is not permissible for hady (sacrificial animal sent to the Haram in Hajj) and udhiyah (Eid sacrifice).
These are ritual acts of slaughter that require a specific animal; cash cannot replace the physical sacrifice.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i and the Shafi'i school hold that paying value is not permissible in any of these obligations.
Their reasoning is that the texts specify the exact item; substituting value is not obedience to the command.
4
Contrast the Hanafi rationale
Hanafis rely on istihsan (juristic preference) and the objective of fulfilling the obligation. Value can equally benefit the poor and is often more practical.
For hady and udhiyah, the objective is the act of slaughter itself, so value cannot substitute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafis allow cash payment for zakat, making distribution efficient; Shafi'is require the exact item, e.g., a sheep or goat.
This difference affects how zakat is collected and distributed in contemporary settings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز دَفع القيمة في الزكاة.
وكذا في النذور، والكفارات والعشر، وصدقة الفطر.
ولا يجوز في الهدايا، والضحايا.
وقال الشافعي(۱): لا يجوز.
(۱) المجموع ٤٢٨/٥.
And it is permissible to pay the value in zakat.
And likewise in nadhr (vows), kaffarat (expiation), ushr (tithe), and zakat al-fitr.
And it is not permissible in hady (sacrificial animals of Hajj) and udhiyah (sacrificial animals of Eid).
And al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about paying the monetary value (qimah) instead of the specified item in various acts of worship. According to the Hanafi school, it is permissible to pay the value in zakat, nadhr, kaffarat, ushr, and sadaqat al-fitr. However, it is not permissible for hady (the sacrificial animal for Hajj) and udhiyah (the Eid sacrifice). The Shafi'i school, represented here by Imam al-Shafi'i, holds that paying value is not permissible in any of these cases. The footnote references al-Majmu' (a Shafi'i source) for al-Shafi'i's position.

Key Terms
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value or cash equivalent of the prescribed item in zakat and other obligations.
هدايا (hadaya): Sacrificial animals sent to the Haram for Hajj (hady).
ضحايا (dahaya): Sacrificial animals slaughtered on Eid al-Adha (udhiyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakah
B) Nadhr (vows)
C) Kaffarat (expiation)
D) Udhiyah (Eid sacrifice)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits paying cash value in financial obligations but not in ritual sacrifices, based on the purpose of the act.

No Zakat on Work, Pregnant, and Fed Animals
Ruling with talil on exemption of certain livestock from zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which categories of livestock are exempt from zakat in the Hanafi school and explain why using the principle of nama' (growth).

Core Idea
Zakat is due only on growing wealth; work, pregnant, and fed animals lack true growth, so no zakat is due.

This lesson clarifies a key Hanafi principle: zakat is linked to natural growth. The text from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explicitly exempts three categories of livestock. We will examine the ruling and the reasoning behind it, focusing on the concept of nama'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exempt categories
List them: work animals (al-‘awāmil), pregnant animals (al-ḥawāmil), and fed animals (al-‘alūfah). These are explicitly stated as exempt from zakat ('la sadaqah').
2
Understand the broadening of 'work animals' and 'fed animals'
The commentary clarifies: work animals are exempt even if they are pastured (asimat); fed animals are exempt even if they are not worked upon. The exemption is not conditional on the absence of other factors.
The primary criterion is the animal's intended use, not its grazing method.
3
Grasp the underlying cause (sabab) for exemption
Zakat is due on growing wealth (mal namī). The indicators of growth are pasturing (isamah) or preparation for trade (i‘dād lil-tijārah). These categories lack genuine growth due to their purpose or cost.
The concept of nama' is central; without it, there is no obligation.
4
Apply the reasoning to each category
For work animals, their labor prevents free growth. For pregnant animals, their condition implies additional care and cost. For fed animals, maintenance expenses accumulate, effectively negating growth.
The reasoning is talīl (legal cause) based on the absence of the condition for zakat.
5
Contrast with potential opposing views
Other schools might impose zakat if these animals are also kept for trade. The Hanafi school maintains a strict separation of purpose; the dominant use determines the ruling.
This distinction highlights the Hanafi emphasis on the effective cause (illah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس في العوامل، والحوامل، والعلوفة صدقةٌ.
يعني بالعوامل ولو أسيمت وبالعلوفة: ولو لم يُعمل عليها؛ لأن السبب هو المال النامي، ودليله الإسامة، أو الإعداد للتجارة، ولم يوجد، ولأن في العلوفة تتراكم المؤنة، فينعدم النماء فيها معنى.
And there is no sadaqah (zakat) on work animals, pregnant animals, and fed animals.
Meaning by work animals: even if they are pastured; and by fed animals: even if they are not worked upon; because the cause [of zakat] is growing wealth, and its indicator is pasturing (isamah) or preparation for trade, and that is not present, and because in fed animals the maintenance expenses accumulate, so growth (nama') is meaningfully absent.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with a commentary explaining the reasoning. The matn states that no zakat is due on three categories: (1) al-‘awāmil: animals used for work (e.g., oxen used for ploughing), (2) al-ḥawāmil: pregnant animals, and (3) al-‘alūfah: animals that are fed and not left to graze freely. The commentary clarifies that even if work animals are pastured (asimat), they are still exempt because the primary use is work. For fed animals, even if they are not worked upon, they are exempt because the cost of feeding accumulates and thus there is no true growth (nama'). The underlying principle is that zakat is due on growing wealth (mal namī), and these categories lack the aspect of natural increase due to their purpose or expense.

Key Terms
عوامل (‘awāmil): Animals used for work such as ploughing or carrying loads.
حوامل (ḥawāmil): Pregnant animals.
علوفة (‘alūfah): Animals that are fed (not left to graze freely).
نامي (nāmī): Growing wealth; wealth that increases naturally or through trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are not owned
B) Because the cost of feeding accumulates and prevents true growth
C) Because they are used for trade
D) Because they are always pregnant

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, zakat is only due on livestock that grow naturally without significant maintenance cost; work, pregnant, and fed animals are exempt.

Collector Must Take Medium Quality, Not Best or Worst
Ruling on quality of zakat animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the quality of zakat animals to be taken by the collector and explain the rationale for the medium quality.

Core Idea
The zakat collector must take medium quality to balance the rights of owner and poor.

This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah explains a key principle in zakat collection: the collector cannot take the best or worst animals, but must take an average. This rule ensures fairness to both the wealthy owner and the needy recipient, preventing harm to either party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the zakat collector selecting animals for zakat. The ruling is that he must take the medium quality (wasat) – it is wajib.
The text states 'he takes the middle' – this is obligatory.
2
Understand the prohibitions
The collector is prohibited from taking the best (khiyar) and the worst (radhilah). These are extremes.
'Khiyar' means the best; 'radhilah' means the worst.
3
Grasp the rationale
The reason ('illa) is to avoid harming both sides: taking the best harms the owner, taking the worst harms the poor. The medium balances their interests.
The commentary explicitly mentions consideration of both parties.
4
Apply the method
The collector should divide the livestock into three categories: good, poor, and medium, then take from the medium. This is the practical implementation.
This three-fold division ensures a fair assessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يأخذ المُصَدِّقُ خيار المال ولا رذالته، ويأخذ الوَسَطَ منه .
قوله: (ولا يأخذ المصدقُ خيار المال ولا رذالته) : أي : ولا رديئه.
* قوله : (ويأخذ الوسط منه) ؛ لأن فيه نظراً من الجانبين؛ لأن في أخذ خياره : إضراراً بأصحاب الأموال، وفي أخذ رذالته: إضراراً بالفقراء، فيقسمه ثلاثة أقسام : جيد، ورديء، ووسط، ويأخذ من الوسط.
And the collector does not take the best (khiyar) of the wealth nor its worst (radhilah), but takes the middle (wasat) from it.
His statement: (And the collector does not take the best of the wealth nor its worst): meaning, nor its inferior.
* His statement: (And he takes the middle from it); because it considers both sides; because taking its best harms the owners, and taking its worst harms the poor. So he divides it into three parts: good, poor, and middle, and takes from the middle.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the quality of animals taken as zakat. The matn instructs that the collector must not take the best animals (khiyar) nor the worst (radhilah), but must take the medium (wasat). The commentary clarifies that 'radhilah' means inferior. The reason is balance: taking the best would harm the owners, taking the worst would harm the poor recipients. Therefore, the property should be divided into three categories—good, poor, and medium—and the collector takes from the medium category.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The best and most valuable animals in the owner's property.
رذالة (radhilah): The worst and least valuable animals.
وسط (wasat): The medium quality; average in value and condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the zakat collector take the medium quality according to the text?
A) Because it is easiest for the collector
B) Because it balances the interests of both the owner and the poor
C) Because it is the most common type
D) Because the owner prefers it

Key Takeaway
The medium quality is the required standard for zakat collection to ensure fairness to both the owner and the poor.

Paying Value (Qimah) in Zakat and Similar Obligations: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
States Hanafi ruling on paying value and notes Shafi'i disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on paying value in zakat and similar obligations, and explain why the Shafi'i school disagrees.

Core Idea
Hanafi permits cash value in zakat; Shafi'i requires the specific commodity.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayira presents a clear ruling: in the Hanafi school, paying the monetary value (qimah) is permissible for zakat and several other obligations, but not for gifts and sacrifices. This practical ruling reflects a deeper usul principle about the purpose of these obligations. Imam al-Shafi'i holds the opposite view, requiring the specific item. Let's unpack the ruling and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of Hanafi permissibility
The text lists where qimah is permitted: zakat, vows, expiations, 'ushr, sadaqat al-fitr. It also lists exceptions: hadaya and adahiya.
Memorize the list of exceptions.
2
Understand the Hanafi rationale
Though not explicit in the passage, the Hanafi view is based on istihsan: the goal is to fulfill the right of the poor, achievable through value.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi principle.
3
Note the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i says it is not permissible in any obligation. The basis is the literal texts specifying the commodity.
Different usul methodology.
4
Determine the practical consequence
A Hanafi can pay cash zakat; a Shafi'i must give the specific animal or food item.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الخيل ويجوز دفع القيمة في الزكاة. وكذا في النذور، والكفارات والعشر، وصدقة الفطر. ولا يجوز في الهدايا، والضحايا. وقال الشافعي(۱): لا يجوز.
Chapter of Zakat of Horses. And it is permissible to pay value in zakat. And likewise in vows, and expiations, and the tithe ('ushr), and zakat of breaking the fast (sadaqat al-fitr). And it is not permissible in gifts (hadaya) and sacrifices (adahiya). And al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on paying value (qimah) instead of the specific commodity in zakat and similar obligations. The text begins by stating the general rule: it is permissible to pay value in zakat. The commentator extends this rule to vows (nudhur), expiations (kaffarat), the tithe on land produce ('ushr), and sadaqat al-fitr (zakat al-fitr). However, it is not permissible in the case of gifts (hadaya) and sacrifices (adahiya), which must be given in kind. The passage then notes the disagreement of Imam al-Shafi'i, who does not permit paying value in any of these obligations. The Hanafi school bases its position on istihsan and the principle that the objective is fulfilling the right of the poor, which can be achieved through value. The Shafi'i school requires the specified commodity because the texts mention specific items. This is a classic inter-madhhab dispute.

Key Terms
القيمة (al-qīmah): Monetary value or market price of the obligatory commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which of the following is paying value (qimah) NOT permissible?
A) Zakat
B) Vows (nudhur)
C) Gifts (hadaya)
D) Expiation (kaffarah)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, paying value is permissible in zakat, vows, expiations, 'ushr, and sadaqat al-fitr, but not in hadaya and adahiya.

Zakat Collector Must Take Medium Quality
Instructs zakat collector on choosing medium quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the quality of property to be taken by the zakat collector and explain the reason behind it.

Core Idea
The zakat collector must take medium-quality property to balance the rights of owners and the poor.

Ensuring fairness in zakat collection is a key principle of Islamic law. This lesson examines a ruling from the Hanafi school that dictates the collector must choose the medium grade of property, neither the best nor the worst. By understanding this ruling, you will appreciate how the law prevents harm (ḍarar) to both the giver and the receiver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the selection of property (e.g., livestock) by the zakat collector. The ruling is that taking medium quality is wajib (obligatory).
The text uses imperative 'ya'khudh' indicating obligation.
2
Identify the prohibited actions
The collector is prohibited from taking the best (khiyār al-māl) and the worst (radhālah). These are the two extremes.
The prohibition is explicit: 'lā ya'khudh'.
3
Understand the reason (ʿillah) behind the prohibition
Taking the best harms the owners by overburdening them; taking the worst harms the poor by giving inferior goods. The medium avoids both harms.
The commentator mentions 'nazar min al-jānibayn' – consideration for both sides.
4
Recognize the method of implementation
The collector should divide the property into three categories: jayyid (good), radī' (bad), and wasaṭ (medium), and take from the middle category.
This ensures a fair average.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يأخذ المصدقُ خيار المال ولا رذالته) : أي : ولا رديئه. * قوله : (ويأخذ الوسط منه) ؛ لأن فيه نظراً من الجانبين؛ لأن في أخذ خياره : إضراراً بأصحاب الأموال، وفي أخذ رذالته: إضراراً بالفقراء، فيقسمه ثلاثة أقسام : جيد، ورديء، ووسط، ويأخذ من الوسط.
He said: (And the collector should not take the best of the property nor its inferior) meaning: nor its defective. * He said: (And he takes the medium from it) because there is consideration for both sides; because taking the best harms the owners, and taking the inferior harms the poor. So he divides it into three categories: good, bad, and medium, and takes from the medium.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage addresses the zakat collector's duty in selecting animals or goods for collection. The base text commands that the collector should not take the best quality (khayār) nor the worst (radhīl). The commentator explains that taking the best harms the owners by overburdening them, while taking the worst harms the poor by giving them inferior quality. Therefore, the collector should divide the property into three grades: good, mediocre, and intermediate, and take from the middle category. This ensures fairness to both parties. This ruling is based on the principle of preventing harm (ḍarar) and achieving balance.

Key Terms
خيار المال (khiyār al-māl): The best and most valuable part of the property due for zakat.
رذالة (radhālah): The inferior or defective part of the property.
الوسط (al-wasaṭ): The medium or average quality between the best and worst.
